Rulings on Hedgehog and Land Reptiles: Qiyas Applied
Extends prohibition via qiyas from hedgehog to all land reptiles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on land reptiles and how qiyas extends the prohibition from the hedgehog.

Core Idea
Land reptiles are haram by analogy to the hedgehog, which is prohibited by hadith.

This lesson continues from the previous one, focusing on the ruling for hedgehog and all land reptiles. The Hanafi school uses a clear qiyas: the hedgehog is a land reptile and its meat is not permissible, therefore all similar land reptiles are haram. We will examine the hadith evidence and the structure of the qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asl (original case) and its ruling
The asl is the hedgehog (قنفذ). Its ruling is that it is not halal, as stated in the hadith. The commentator notes it is makruh or haram.
2
Identify the far' (new case)
The far' is all land reptiles (حشرات الارض), such as mice, turtles, snakes, scorpions.
3
Determine the illa (effective cause)
The illa is being a land reptile that is considered khabith (filthy) and not generally edible. The hedgehog shares this characteristic.
The commentator explicitly states: 'the hedgehog is a land reptile' and uses this to draw the analogy.
4
Derive the hukm for the far'
Since the illa is present in all land reptiles, the ruling of prohibition (haram) applies to them as well.
5
Verify the soundness of the qiyas
The Hanafi school validates this qiyas because the illa is clear (jaliyyah) and shared. No objections are recorded in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۹۱۹ (۴۰) ولا يجوز اكل لحم الحمر الاهلية والبغال [۲۲۰ (۴۱) ویکره اکل ماروی فی القنفذ وحشرات الارض ج تاسع ص ۵۴۷ نمبر ۱۹۴۳۱) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ قنفذ یعنی جنگلی چوہا کھانا ٹھیک نہیں ہے اور وہ حشرات الارض میں سے ہے اس لئے حشرات الارض کا کھانا بھی حلال نہیں ہے (۳) گوہ بھی حشرات الارض میں سے ہے وہ اوپر کی حدیث میں مکروہ بلکہ حرام کہا ہے اس لئے اس پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے باقی حشرات الارض بھی حرام ہیں۔ الضبع : بجو الضب : گوہ۔ حشرات الارض : زمین پر رینگنے والے جانور جیسے چوہا، کچھوا، سانپ ، بچھو وغیرہ۔ [۲۶۱۹] (۴۰) اور جائز نہیں ہے گھریلو گدھوں کا کھانا اور خچر کو کھانا۔ ایک جنگلی گدھا ہوتا ہے جس کو کھانا حلال ہے اور ایک پالتو گدھا ہوتا ہے جس کا کھانا حرام ہے۔ اور گدھا اور گھوڑی دونوں کے ملاپ سے جو بچہ پیدا ہوتا ہے اس کو خچر کہتے ہیں وہ بھی حرام ہے۔ عن ابن عمر نهى النبي الي الله عن لحوم الحمر الاهلية يوم خيبر (الف) ( بخاری شریف ، باب لحوم الحمر الانسة ص ۸۲۹ نمبر ۵۵۲۱ مسلم شریف ، باب تحریم اکل لحم الحمر الانسة، ص ۱۴۹ نمبر ۱۹۳۶) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ گھریلو گدھے کا گوشت حلال نہیں ہے ۔ پہلے جائز تھا، جنگ خیبر میں حرام ہو گیا۔ اور خچر کا گوشت حرام ہے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن خالد بن الوليد ان رسول الله الله نهي عن اكل لحوم الخيل والبغال والحمير وكل ذي ناب من السباع (ب) (نسائی شریف، باب تحریم اکل لحوم الخیل ص ۶۰۲ نمبر ۴۳۳۷ رابن ماجہ شریف ، باب لحوم البغال ص ۴۶۴ نمبر ۳۱۹۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا خچر کا گوشت حلال نہیں ہے (۲) نچر گدھے کی پیداوار ہے جب گدھا حلال نہیں ہے تو خچر حلال کیسے ہو گا ؟

حاشیہ : (پچھلے صفحہ سے آگے ) کے کھانے کے بارے میں پوچھا تو انہوں نے یہ آیت پڑھی قل لا اجد الخ ۔ حضرت ابو ہریرہ نے فرمایا حضور کے سامنے چوہے کا تذکرہ ہوا تو آپ نے فرمایا خبیثوں میں سے ایک خبیث ہے (الف) آپ نے اپلی گدھے کے گوشت سے خیبر کے دن منع فرمایا (ب) آپ نے روکا گھوڑے، خچر اور گدھے کے گوشت کھانے سے اور پھاڑ کھانے والے نوکیلے دانت والے جانور کے کھانے سے (ج) حضور فرماتے ہیں کہ نہیں حلال ہے گھوڑا ، گدھا اور خچر کا
گوشت۔
Ruling (40): It is not permissible to eat domestic donkeys and mules. Ruling (41): It is disliked to eat hedgehog and land reptiles. From this hadith: hedgehog (wild mouse) is not permissible, and it is a land reptile, therefore eating land reptiles is also not halal. The goat (dabb) is also a land reptile; in the above hadith it is called makruh or haram, so by qiyas the rest of land reptiles are haram. Definitions: Dabb: Goat. Land reptiles: crawling animals like mice, turtles, snakes, scorpions etc. Then commentary for (40): It is not permissible to eat domestic donkeys and mules. The wild donkey is halal, but the domestic donkey is haram. The hybrid of a donkey and horse (mule) is also haram. Hadith from Ibn Umar: The Prophet forbade domestic donkey meat at Khaybar. This shows that domestic donkey meat is not halal; it was permissible before but forbidden at Khaybar. Mule meat is haram, with evidence from Khalid ibn al-Walid hadith forbidding horse, mule, donkey, and every fanged beast. Footnote: (previous page continued) He was asked about eating it, and he recited the verse 'Qul la ajid'... Abu Hurairah said the Prophet commented on mice saying it is one of the filthy things. (a) He forbade domestic donkey meat at Khaybar. (b) He forbade horse, mule, donkey and fanged beasts. (c) He said it is not halal: horse, donkey, and mule meat.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage from Al-Sharh al-Thumayri covers Kitab al-Sayd. The author begins with the matn ruling that it is not permissible to eat domestic donkeys and mules. The commentator explains that wild donkeys are halal but domestic are haram, and mules (offspring of donkey and horse) are also haram. He then cites hadith: Ibn Umar reported the Prophet forbade domestic donkey meat at Khaybar; another hadith from Khalid ibn al-Walid explicitly forbids horse, mule, and donkey meat as well as every beast with fangs. The commentator then discusses the ruling on hedgehog (qunfudh) which is makruh according to a narration, and extends this via qiyas to all land reptiles (hasharat al-ard), making them haram. The reasoning is that the hedgehog is a land reptile, and the hadith indicates it is not halal, so by analogy all such creatures are prohibited. The author also defines terms: al-dabb (goat) and hasharaat al-ard (creeping animals like mice, turtles, snakes, scorpions). This section shows how Hanafi fiqh uses textual evidence and qiyas to derive rulings on unusual animals. The footnote on page 38 further strengthens the prohibition with additional hadith.

Key Terms
الحشرات (al-hasharat): Land reptiles and creeping animals like mice, turtles, snakes, scorpions.
القنفذ (al-qunfudh): Hedgehog, a spiny mammal considered a land reptile in the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider all land reptiles haram?
A) Because they are explicitly mentioned in the Quran.
B) By analogy (qiyas) to the hedgehog, which is prohibited by hadith.
C) Because they have fangs.
D) Because they are not slaughtered.

Key Takeaway
Land reptiles are haram by analogy to the hedgehog, which is prohibited by hadith.

Ruling on Horse Meat: Understanding the Hanafi Position of Karahah
Presents Hanafi ruling of karahah and Shafi'i/Sahibayn ruling of halal with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on horse meat (makruh), identify the two conflicting hadith proofs, and evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions.

Core Idea
Horse meat is halal but makruh in the Hanafi school due to jihad necessity, while the Sahibayn and Shafi'i hold it is halal without karahah.

This passage presents an important ikhtilaf regarding the permissibility of horse meat. Imam Abu Hanifa considers it makruh (disliked) due to a valid public interest – preserving horses for jihad. However, Imam al-Shafi'i and the two senior Hanafi disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, consider it halal based on direct prophetic permission. Understanding this dispute clarifies how different considerations (maslahah vs. explicit text) can lead to divergent rulings within and across madhahib.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on horse meat
Identify exactly what Imam Abu Hanifa rules: horse meat is halal but makruh. Note that makruh does not mean haram; it is a disliked category.
The ruling is based on a secondary reason (jihad), not intrinsic impurity.
2
Identify the dalil used by Imam Abu Hanifa
Locate the hadith of Khalid ibn al-Walid that the Prophet said: 'لَا يَحِلُّ أَكْلُ لُحُومِ الْخَيْلِ وَالْبِغَالِ وَالْحَمِيرِ' (It is not halal to eat the meat of horses, mules, and donkeys). This hadith appears in Nasa'i and Ibn Majah.
This is a dalil naqli (textual evidence) that Imam Abu Hanifa uses to support karahah.
3
Identify the opposing position and evidence
Imam al-Shafi'i and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that horse meat is halal. They cite the hadith of Jabir that the Prophet forbade donkey meat at Khaybar but permitted horse meat, and that the Companions ate horse and wild donkey meat.
The opposing evidence includes both prophetic permission (رخصة) and actual Sahaba practice.
4
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is in the hukm level: makruh tanzihi vs. halal. A follower of Imam Abu Hanifa may avoid horse meat out of caution, while a follower of the Sahibayn considers it permissible without any dislike.
The mufta bihi (operative position) in the Hanafi school today, as per later scholars like Ibn Abidin, follows the Sahibayn: halal.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and modern application
Later Hanafi scholars give more weight to the hadith of Jabir due to its authenticity and the practice of the Sahaba. The original jihad concern is less relevant today, so the caution is no longer necessary.
Most contemporary Hanafi fatwas consider horse meat halal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۶۲۰] (۴۱) مکروہ ہے گھوڑے کا گوشت کھانا امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ گھوڑے کا گوشت حلال ہے تا ہم مکروہ ہے۔ یہ جہاد میں کام آتا ہے اس کو کھا نا عام کر دیا جائے تو جہاد کا نقصان ہوگا اس لئے اس کو مکروہ قرار دیا ہے (۲) او پر حدیث میں گزرا عن خالد بن وليد انه سمع رسول الله الا الله يقول لا يحل اكل لحوم الخيل والبغال والحمير (ج) ( نسائی شریف، باب تحریم اكل لحوم الخیل ص ۶۰۲ نمبر ۴۳۳۶ رابن ماجه شریف، باب لحوم البغال ص ۴۶۴ نمبر ۳۱۹۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ گھوڑا کھانا حرام ہے۔ اور امام شافعی اور صاحبین سفر ماتے ہیں کہ گھوڑا کھانا حلال ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ عن جابر بن عبد الله قال نهى النبي الا الله يوم خيبر عن لحوم الحمر ورخص في لحوم الخيل لحم الفرس عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب لحوم الخیل ص ۸۲۹ نمبر ۵۵۲۰ر مسلم شریف، باب اباحته اكل لحوم الخیل ص ۱۵۰ نمبر ۱۹۴۱) اسی کی دوسری روایت میں یہ بھی ہے۔ سمع جابر بن عبد الله يقول اكلنا زمن خيبر ا الخيل وحمر الوحش (ب) ( مسلم شریف، نمبر ۵۰۲۳/۱۹۴۱/ بخاری شریف، نمبر ۵۵۱۹) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ گھوڑے کا گوشت کھانا حلال ہے۔
Number (41): It is disliked to eat horse meat according to Imam Abu Hanifa. Horse meat is permissible but disliked. The reason is that horses are used in jihad, and if eating them becomes common, jihad would be harmed. There is a hadith from Khalid ibn al-Walid that the Prophet said it is not permissible to eat horse, mule, and donkey meat, and every beast with fangs. From this hadith it is understood that horse meat is haram. Imam Shafi'i and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that horse meat is halal. There is a hadith from Jabir that the Prophet forbade donkey meat at Khaybar but permitted horse meat. The text then says 'Horse meat according to Abu Hanifa' and cites Bukhari and Muslim. Another narration from Jabir says 'We ate horses and wild donkeys at Khaybar.' So this hadith shows that horse meat is halal.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This section presents the intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab dispute on horse meat. Imam Abu Hanifa rules it makruh (disliked) due to a practical reason: horses are needed for jihad, and widespread consumption would deplete military resources. He also cites the hadith of Khalid that explicitly forbids horse meat. However, Imam Shafi'i and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold it is halal, based on the hadith of Jabir that the Prophet permitted horse meat at Khaybar while forbidding donkey. They also rely on the practice of the Companions eating horse meat. The commentator notes that Imam Abu Hanifa's view is one of caution, but the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that horse meat is halal but makruh tanzihi? Actually the text says makruh, but many later Hanafi scholars consider it halal due to the strength of the evidence from Jabir. The dispute shows how different evidences are weighed.

Key Terms
مكروه (makruh): In this context, it means religiously disliked, but not haram. The author explains it is because of a secondary reason (jihad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Abu Hanifa consider horse meat makruh?
A) Because it is mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because horses are used for jihad and eating them would harm military capability.
C) Because it is a land reptile.
D) Because it has not been slaughtered.

Key Takeaway
Horse meat is considered makruh by Imam Abu Hanifa due to jihad concerns, but the relied-upon Hanafi position (Sahibayn) and Shafi'i school consider it halal.

Permissibility of Eating Rabbit: Proof and Ruling
States that eating rabbit is permissible with supporting hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating rabbit and explain the dalil (proof) from the hadith.

Core Idea
Rabbit is halal because it is herbivorous and the Prophet accepted its meat – clear evidence of permissibility.

This lesson addresses the permissibility of eating rabbit, a common question. The Hanafi school rules it halal based on two grounds: its nature as a herbivore and a direct hadith. Let's examine the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act of eating rabbit. The ruling is mubah (permissible).
In Hanafi terminology, halal often means mubah.
2
Locate the dalil
The source cites a hadith from Bukhari and Muslim: Anas reports that a rabbit was caught, slaughtered, and the Prophet accepted its meat.
The hadith is ahad, but sufficient for establishing permissibility.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's acceptance indicates approval, which in Hanafi usul implies ibaha (permissibility).
This is based on the principle that the Prophet's tacit approval (taqrir) is a form of sunnah.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is no significant disagreement among the schools; all consider rabbit halal.
Some minor differences may exist regarding conditions of slaughter.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The ruling is straightforward: the hadith is authentic and the reasoning from the animal's nature supports it.
No tarjih needed as there is no internal dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۶۲۱] (۴۲) ولا بأس باكل الارنب خرگوش نه گوشت خور جانور ہے اور نہ گندگی کھاتا ہے۔ وہ گھاس کھانے والا جانور ہے اس لئے وہ حلال ہوگا (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن انس قال انفجنا ارنبا ونحن بمر الظهر ان فسعى القوم فلغبوا فاخذتها فجئت بها الى ابي طلحة فذبحها فبعث بوركيها او قال بفخذيها الى النبي الله فقبلها (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب الارنب ص ۸۳۰ نمبر ۵۵۳۵ر مسلم شریف ، باب اباحه الارنب ص ۱۵۲ نمبر ۱۹۵۳) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ خرگوش حلال ہے۔
Number (42): There is no harm in eating rabbit. The rabbit is neither a carnivore nor does it eat filth. It is a herbivore, so it will be halal. In a hadith, Anas said: We startled a rabbit at Marr al-Zahran, the people chased it and tired, then I caught it and brought it to Abu Talha who slaughtered it. He sent its thighs to the Prophet, and the Prophet accepted it. This hadith shows that rabbit is halal.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This brief ruling states that rabbit is halal. The commentator provides two reasons: first, rabbit is not a meat-eater nor does it consume impurities; it eats grass, so it is considered pure and permissible. Second, there is a direct hadith from Anas where a rabbit was hunted and presented to the Prophet, and he accepted it, indicating approval. This is a clear proof of permissibility.

Key Terms
الأرنب (al-arnab): Rabbit, a herbivorous animal, permissible to eat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a reason given in this lesson for rabbit being halal?
A) It is a herbivore.
B) It does not eat filth.
C) The Prophet accepted rabbit meat.
D) It is mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Rabbit is halal because it is a herbivore and the Prophet's acceptance of its meat confirms its permissibility.

6 min
Permissibility of Eating Rabbit
Ruling with hadith proof for permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating rabbit and explain the hadith evidence that establishes its permissibility.

Core Idea
Rabbit is halal because it is herbivorous; the Prophet's acceptance of rabbit meat confirms it.

In this lesson, we examine a clear ruling from the Hanafi school: eating rabbit is permissible (mubah). The author provides both a rational basis ('the rabbit is not a carnivore') and a direct textual proof from the Sunnah. Understanding how this proof works is essential for evaluating similar cases of animals not explicitly mentioned in the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on eating rabbit. Note the term 'la ba's' which indicates permissibility (ibahah) in Hanafi fiqh.
La ba's can sometimes imply slight dislike, but here context makes halal clear.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author cites a hadith from Anas: the Prophet (peace be upon him) accepted rabbit meat sent to him. Identify that this is a hadith ahad (single narration) from Bukhari and Muslim.
Hadith is ahad but sahih; it is sufficient for fiqh rulings.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof proves the ruling)
The Prophet's acceptance (qabalaha) is a tacit approval (taqrir). In usul, taqrir by the Prophet indicates permissibility. Therefore, eating rabbit is halal.
Taqrir is a valid proof of permissibility when there is no prohibition.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Some early scholars considered rabbit makruh or even haram, possibly by analogy with carnivores. The Hanafi school responds by distinguishing based on diet and the specific hadith.
The hadith overrides any analogical objection.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that rabbit is halal (mubah). This is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is the view taken for fatwa.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is recorded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٤٢) ولا بأس بأكل الأرنب [٢٦٢٢] لا حرج في أكل الأرنب. الأرنب ليس بحيوان لاحم ولا يأكل النجاسة، بل هو من الحيوانات العشبية، لذا فهو حلال. في الحديث: عن أنس قال: انفجنا أرنبا ونحن بمر الظهران فسعى القوم فلغبوا فأخذتها فجئت بها إلى أبي طلحة فذبحها فبعث بوركيها أو قال بفخذيها إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقبلها. (رواه البخاري ومسلم) دليل على حل الأرنب. حاشية (ج): عن أنس قال: انفجنا أرنبا... فقبلها.
(42) And there is no harm in eating the rabbit [2622]. There is no prohibition in eating rabbit. The rabbit is not a carnivorous animal nor does it eat filth; rather it is a herbivorous animal, so it is halal. In the hadith: Anas said: We flushed out a rabbit while we were at Marr al-Zahran, and the people ran after it and got tired, so I caught it and brought it to Abu Talhah, who slaughtered it. He sent its thighs or its haunches to the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he accepted it. (Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim) Evidence for the permissibility of rabbit. Footnote (j): Anas said: We flushed out a rabbit... and he accepted it.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the permissibility of eating rabbit. The author states unequivocally that there is no harm (la ba's) in eating rabbit, which is a term indicating permissibility (ibahah) in Hanafi fiqh. The reasoning provided is that the rabbit is not a carnivore (laysa bi-hayawan lahhm) and does not eat filth (la ya'kul al-najasah); rather it feeds on grass, so it falls under the category of herbivorous land animals which are generally halal. The proof is a hadith from Anas ibn Malik (may Allah be pleased with him) where the Prophet (peace be upon him) accepted rabbit meat, establishing its permissibility. The Hanafi position is clear: rabbit is halal.

Key Terms
لا بأس (la ba's): A term indicating that an action is permissible (mubah) and there is no legal harm in doing it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Only Fish is Halal from Aquatic Animals
Ruling that among aquatic animals, only fish is permissible to eat; all others are haram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on aquatic animals and explain how the hadith specifies the general Qur'anic verse.

Core Idea
Only fish is halal from the sea — the hadith of 'two dead things' specifies the general verse.

This passage from Al-Sharh al-Sameri presents the Hanafi ruling that only fish is permissible among aquatic animals. It contrasts this with the Shafi'i position, which permits all sea creatures, and resolves the apparent conflict through the principle of takhsis (specification). Understanding this dispute is essential for grasping how Hanafi usul al-fiqh prioritizes a specific hadith over a general Qur'anic command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the ruling clearly: only fish (samak) is halal; all other aquatic animals are haram according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
This includes shrimp, crab, lobster, frog, etc.
2
Examine the Hanafi dalil
Read the hadith of fish and locust (Ibn Majah) and the hadith prohibiting killing frogs (Abu Dawud). Understand how these serve as evidence.
The first hadith explicitly mentions fish; the second implies other sea creatures are not permissible.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position and dalil
Recall that Imam al-Shafi'i holds all sea animals are halal, citing the Qur'anic verse (5:96) 'Lawful to you is game from the sea and its food.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi response: takhsis
Apply the usul principle that a general text (amm) can be specified by a specific text (khass). The verse is general, the hadith is specific, so the hadith limits the verse to fish only.
This is a key usul al-fiqh concept.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: Hanafi Muslims only eat fish from the sea; Shafi'i Muslims eat all seafood. This difference affects daily dietary choices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٤٤) ولا يؤكل من حيوان الماء إلا السمك. عند الإمام أبي حنيفة: من حيوان البحر لا يحل إلا السمك، والباقي حرام. حديث: عن عبد الله بن عمر أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: أحلت لنا ميتتان: الحوت والجراد. (رواه ابن ماجه) دل على أن الحوت (السمك) فقط حلال. وحديث: منع الضفدع في الدواء، فعلم أن ما سوى السمك حرام. عن عبد الرحمن بن عثمان: أن طبيبا سأل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن ضفدع يجعلها في دواء فنهاه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن قتلها. (رواه أبو داود) فهذا دليل على أن غير السمك ليس بحلال. الإمام الشافعي: كل حيوان البحر حلال، لآية: أحل لكم صيد البحر وطعامه. (المائدة 96) وفي الحديث: عن أبي هريرة... والجواب: الآية عامة مخصوصة بالحديث.
(44) And from aquatic animals, nothing is eaten except fish. According to Imam Abu Hanifa: from sea animals, only fish is halal; the rest are haram. Hadith: Abdullah ibn Umar reported that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Two dead things have been made lawful for us: fish and locust.' (Ibn Majah) This indicates that only fish (al-hut) is halal. And the hadith: He forbade the frog in medicine, so it is known that other than fish is haram. From Abd al-Rahman ibn Uthman: A doctor asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about using a frog in medicine, and the Prophet forbade killing it. (Abu Dawud) This is evidence that other than fish is not halal. Imam al-Shafi'i: All sea animals are halal, for the verse: 'Lawful to you is game from the sea and its food.' (Al-Ma'idah 5:96) And in the hadith from Abu Hurayrah... The response: The verse is general but specified by the hadith.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on aquatic animals followed by an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, states that only fish (samak) from sea creatures is halal; all other aquatic animals are haram. Two proofs are given: (1) The hadith 'Two dead things have been made lawful: fish and locust' — this indicates that only fish is permissible among sea creatures because it is specifically mentioned. (2) The Prophet forbade killing frogs even for medicinal use, which shows that frogs are not to be used, implying they are not halal to eat; by analogy, other aquatic animals besides fish are similarly prohibited. The Shafi'i position is then mentioned: all sea animals are halal based on the Qur'anic verse 'Lawful to you is game from the sea and its food' (Surah al-Ma'idah, 5:96) and a hadith from Abu Hurayrah. The Hanafi response is that the verse is general ('amm) but has been specified (takhsis) by the hadith, particularly the hadith of fish and locust, which limits the general permission to fish only. The text does not fully elaborate the response, but the principle of takhsis al-'amm bi al-sunnah is invoked.

Key Terms
ميتتان (maytatān): Two dead things; referring to animals that die without slaughter but are still lawful to eat: fish and locust.
تخصيص (takhsis): Specification of a general text (amm) by a specific text (khass).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Qur'anic verse about sea game.
B) The hadith that two dead things are lawful: fish and locust.
C) The consensus of the Companions.
D) The fact that other sea animals are harmful.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, only fish from aquatic animals is halal; all other sea creatures are haram, based on the hadith specifying the general Qur'anic permission.

Only Fish from Sea Animals is Halal
Ruling that only fish among sea creatures is permissible to eat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sea creatures, cite the primary evidence, and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits only fish from the sea; all other sea creatures are haram.

This lesson addresses an important dietary ruling in the Hanafi school: what seafood is permissible? While many Muslims assume all sea creatures are halal, the Hanafi school holds a specific restriction. We will explore the evidence from the Sunnah and the interpretive differences with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by identifying the exact ruling: only fish from sea animals is halal; all others (crabs, shrimp, lobsters, etc.) are haram.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i holds all sea creatures are halal. Understanding both sides is key to the dispute.
This ikhtilaf is based on different interpretations of primary texts.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: Quran 5:96 ('game of the sea') and the hadith 'its water is pure, its dead are halal.'
These texts appear general in permission.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school specifies these texts using other hadiths: the hadith of two dead things (fish and locust) and the prohibition of killing frogs. This shows only fish is exempt from the prohibition of dead animals.
The Hanafi methodology prioritizes specific over general when conflict arises.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis avoid all non-fish seafood; Shafi'is eat all. This difference affects daily life and fatwa.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi in the school.

[2623] (44) And none of the water animals will be eaten except fish. Imam Abu Hanifa says: Only fish from sea animals is halal; all the rest are haram. There is a hadith: from Abdullah ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Two dead things have been made lawful for us: fish and locust.' This hadith indicates that only fish is halal. (2) There is a hadith: Prohibition of putting frogs in medicine, even though it is a sea animal, indicating that except fish, all other sea animals are haram. The hadith: from Abd al-Rahman ibn Uthman that a doctor asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about a frog to put in medicine, and he forbade killing it. This hadith forbids killing the frog and putting it in medicine, which shows it is not halal; similarly, other sea animals are not halal. According to Imam Shafi'i, all sea animals are halal. There is a verse: 'Lawful to you is the game of the sea and its food, a provision for you and for the travelers' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:96). This verse declares all sea game halal, showing that all sea animals are halal. There is a hadith: Abu Hurairah heard ... then the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Its water is pure, and its dead are halal.' This hadith declares all dead animals from the sea halal, meaning that if sea animals die and are not slaughtered, they are all halal. In some narrations, sea dogs and pigs are haram because they are impure on land, so they will not be halal in the sea either. In Dar Qutni: from Jabir that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no creature in the sea except that Allah has made it halal for the children of Adam.'
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the permissibility of eating sea creatures other than fish. The Hanafi position is that only fish is halal; all other sea animals are haram. The commentator provides evidence: the hadith of the two dead things (fish and locust) indicates that only these are exceptions to the prohibition of dead animals. Additionally, the prohibition against killing frogs (a sea creature) implies they are not halal for consumption. Imam al-Shafi'i argues based on the Quranic verse (5:96) which apparently permits all game from the sea, and the hadith declaring sea water pure and its dead halal. The Hanafi response is that the verse refers to fish specifically (as 'game' and 'food' in the context of the sea). The hadith about sea water purity and dead animals is interpreted to mean fish, as other sea creatures are not considered 'game' in the same sense. Some narrations except sea dogs and pigs due to their impurity on land, further limiting the general permission. Thus, the Hanafi school restricts the permissibility to fish only.

Key Terms
صيد البحر (sayd al-bahr): Game of the sea; sea creatures that can be hunted or caught.
ميتة (maytah): An animal that died without being slaughtered in a lawful manner.

Makruh to Eat Floating Dead Fish (Tafi)
Ruling that eating tafi fish is makruh with hadith proof and exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating tafi fish, identify the dalil from hadith, and distinguish between permissible and makruh conditions.

Core Idea
Eating fish that floats dead on water is makruh due to spoilage, but fish that died from an accident remains permissible.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on consuming fish found floating dead on water (tafi). The passage presents a hadith prohibition and clarifies that the ruling is based on spoilage, not mere death. Understanding this distinction helps apply the ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is eating fish that floats dead on water. The Hanafi ruling is that it is makruh (disliked).
This ruling applies specifically to tafi fish, not all dead fish.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah: the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited eating fish that is floating dead.
The hadith is a sunnah ahad, so the ruling is not definitive prohibition but makruh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition is linked to the fish being spoiled (swollen and rotten). The hadith explicitly says 'do not eat it' for floating dead fish, indicating dislike due to harm.
The reason (illah) is spoilage, not death per se.
4
Note the exception
If the fish died from an accident (e.g., boat collision) and has not spoiled, it is permissible because the illah is absent.
The phrase 'eat what the sea casts out' refers to fish that are fresh.
5
Evaluate the position
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi in the school. Some other schools may consider it halal if not spoiled, but Hanafis stick to the apparent meaning of the hadith.
This ruling is specific to fish; other sea creatures are not halal anyway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۶۲۴] (۴۵) اور مکروہ ہے اس مچھلی کا کھانا جو اوپر تیر جائے۔ پانی میں قدرتی اسباب سے مچھلی مرکز پانی پر تیرنے لگتی ہے اس کو طافی مچھلی کہتے ہیں اس کا کھانا مکروہ ہے۔ عموماً مچھلی مر کر پانی پر تیرنے لگتی ہے جو پھول جاتی ہے اور سڑنے لگتی ہے اس لئے ایسی مچھلی کا کھانا مکروہ قرار دیا۔ (۲) عن جابر بن عبد الله عن النبي الله قال كلوا ما حسر عنه البحر وما القاه وما وجدتموه ميتا او طافيا فوق الماء فلا تاكلوه (ج) (دارقطنی، كتاب الاشربة ج رابع ص ۱۸۰ نمبر ۲۲۶۸ رسنن بیہقی ، باب من كره اكل الکافی ج تاسع ص ۲۲۸ نمبر ۱۸۹۹۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ مرکز پانی پر تیرنے والی مچھلی نہ کھائے۔ جو مچھلی ابھی ابھی مری ہو یا کسی حادثہ کے شکار سے مری ہو اس کو کھا سکتا ہے کیونکہ وہ ابھی سڑی نہیں ہے۔ كلوا ما حسر عنه البحر وما القاہ سے اس کا اشارہ ہے۔ الطافی : وہ مچھلی جو پانی میں مرکر پانی پر تیرنے لگی ہو۔
[2624] (45) And it is makruh to eat the fish that floats on top. In water, from natural causes, a fish dies and begins to float on the water; it is called 'tafi' fish. Eating it is makruh. Generally, when a fish dies and floats on the water, it swells and begins to rot, so eating such fish is deemed makruh. (2) From Jabir ibn Abdullah from the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Eat what the sea casts out and what it throws up, and what you find dead or floating on the water, do not eat it.' This hadith indicates: do not eat the fish that floats on the water. A fish that has just died or died from an accident can be eaten because it has not yet rotted. The phrase 'eat what the sea casts out and what it throws up' refers to that. Al-Tafi: a fish that dies in water and then floats on the surface.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling that it is makruh (disliked) to eat fish that is found floating dead on the surface of the water. Such fish is called 'tafi'. The reason is that fish that dies and floats typically spoils and rots, making it potentially harmful. The commentator provides a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah: the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted eating what the sea casts out but prohibited eating fish found dead floating on the water. However, the commentator clarifies that fish that has just died (e.g., from an accident) and has not yet spoiled can be eaten; the prohibition only applies when the fish is floating due to natural death and decay. This ruling is specific to fish (since sea animals other than fish are not halal anyway).

Key Terms
طافي (tafi): A fish that dies in water and floats on the surface.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is haram

Key Takeaway
Eating tafi fish is makruh, but fish that dies from an accident and is fresh is permissible; the proof is the hadith of Jabir.

Eel and Snake-Like Fish Are Permissible
Ruling with clarification that eel and snake-fish are halal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eel and snake-fish and identify the key criterion (being a fish) that makes them halal.

Core Idea
Eel and snake-fish are halal because they are fish, despite their snake-like appearance.

This lesson covers a simple but important ruling: the permissibility of eating certain fish that resemble snakes. The Hanafi school, basing on the general permissibility of fish from the sea, affirms that eel (jareeth) and snake-fish (mar mahi) are halal to consume. This lesson reinforces the principle that legal rulings are based on actual classification, not superficial resemblance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the creatures
Recognize that jareeth (eel) and mar mahi (snake-fish) are types of fish, though they look like snakes. The passage explains their appearance but confirms they are fish.
Both terms refer to fish species: jareeth is an eel, mar mahi is a snake-like fish also called bam fish.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule for fish
In the Hanafi school, all fish from the sea are halal. Since these creatures are classified as fish, they are permissible to eat. The shape does not affect the ruling.
The ruling is based on biological classification, not external resemblance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۶۲۵] (۴۶) اور چکی اور بام مچھلی کھانے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے۔ سچکی ایک قسم کی مچھلی ہے جو عام مچھلیوں سے الگ ہوتی ہے یہ بام مچھلی سے تھوڑی الگ شکل کی ہوتی ہے اور سانپ جیسی لمبی ہوتی ہے۔ اس طرح بام مچھلی بھی سانپ کی طرح لمبی ہوتی ہے لیکن وہ مچھلی ہی ہے اس لئے اس کے کھانے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے۔ الجريث : سچکی پچھلی۔ المار ماہی : فارسی لفظ ہے سانپ کی طرح کی پچھلی جس کو ہندوستان میں بام مچھلی کہتے ہیں۔
[2625] (46) And there is no harm in eating eel (jareeth) and snake-fish (mar mahi). 'Sachki' is a type of fish that differs from ordinary fish; it is slightly different in form from 'bam' fish and is long like a snake. Similarly, 'bam' fish is also long like a snake, but it is a fish, so there is no harm in eating it. Al-Jareeth: sachki fish. Al-Mar Mahi: a Persian word for a snake-like fish, called 'bam' fish in India.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a simple ruling that two types of fish — eel (jareeth) and snake-fish (mar mahi) — are permissible to eat. Although these fish resemble snakes in shape, they are true fish, not snakes. The commentator clarifies that despite their unusual appearance, they are categorized as fish and thus fall under the general permissibility of fish. In the Hanafi school, since only fish from sea animals is halal, these creatures are considered fish and therefore halal. The passage provides descriptive reassurance to those who might doubt their edibility due to their snake-like form.

Key Terms
الجريث (al-jareeth): A type of eel; a fish that is long and resembles a snake.
المار ماهی (al-mar mahi): Snake-fish; a Persian term for a snake-like fish.

Locust is Halal Without Slaughter
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on locust and explain the hadith evidence that makes it halal without slaughter.

Core Idea
Locust is halal without slaughter, exempted from the prohibition of dead land animals.

You may wonder whether insects can be eaten in Islam. The Hanafi school addresses this with a specific ruling on locust. In this lesson, we examine the proof that locust is halal without needing slaughter, based on a hadith of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is eating locust. The ruling is that it is halal and slaughter is not required.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? A hadith narrated by Ibn Abi Awfa that the Companions ate locust during expeditions with the Prophet (peace be upon him).
This is a hadith ahad, but sufficient for establishing the ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this hadith prove the ruling? The Companions' practice of eating locust without slaughter demonstrates that it is permissible, and the Prophet's tacit approval confirms it.
The absence of any objection from the Prophet indicates permissibility.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Is there any significant disagreement? No; this ruling is muttafaq alayh across the schools.
It is an exception to the general rule against dead land animals.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi position
Why is this ruling firmly held? It aligns with the principle that two things are halal without slaughter: fish and locust.
The hadith is explicit, and there is no contrary evidence.

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that locust is halal to eat and does not require any form of slaughter (dhabh) to make it lawful. The commentator provides evidence from a hadith narrated by Ibn Abi Awfa, who reports that they ate locust during expeditions with the Prophet (peace be upon him). Since locust is an insect that cannot be slaughtered in the conventional sense, it is specifically exempted. This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that dead land animals are generally haram, but locust is an exception, along with fish. The hadith mentioned earlier in the lesson about two dead things (fish and locust) also supports this.

Key Terms

Ruling on Floating Fish (al-Taafi)
States ruling on floating fish with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating floating fish (al-taafi) and explain the hadith evidence and its interpretation as makruh.

Core Idea
A fish found dead and floating is makruh because its cause of death is unknown and spoilage is likely.

The permissibility of eating fish is generally broad, but the Hanafi school makes a specific distinction for fish that die in water and float to the surface (al-taafi). This lesson examines the ruling and the hadith evidence that supports it, clarifying why the Hanafi jurists classify it as makruh rather than completely forbidden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine the specific act being ruled upon (eating floating fish) and the hukm level (makruh). Note that the hadith uses a prohibition, but the Hanafi school categorises it as makruh tanzihi (disliked) rather than haram.
The term 'makruh' in Hanafi fiqh generally means makruh tanzihi unless specified otherwise.
2
Locate the dalil
Identify the proof: the hadith narrated by Jabir ibn Abdullah in which the Prophet ﷺ distinguishes between fish cast ashore (halal) and those found dead and floating (prohibited).
This is a hadith ahad, not mutawatir, which affects its certainty.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyse how the hadith yields the ruling. The explicit phrase 'fala taakuluh' (do not eat it) indicates avoidance. The Hanafis interpret this as makruh because the hadith is ahad and there may be other evidence allowing fish that die without spoilage, though this is not the relied-upon position.
The school balances the general permissibility of sea creatures with this specific prohibition.
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some other schools may consider floating fish permissible if there is no sign of spoilage, but the Hanafi school holds it as makruh based on the literal wording of the hadith and the potential for harm.
The disagreement is a legitimate ikhtilaf in istinbat from the same evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding fish that die in water and float to the surface (taafi). The Hanafi position is that such fish are makruh to eat. The text provides a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah (may Allah be pleased with him) in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) distinguishes between fish that are cast ashore or caught alive (which are halal) and those found dead and floating—these are not to be eaten. The underlying reasoning is that a floating fish likely died from natural causes and may have started to spoil, posing a health risk. This ruling is specific to fish that die in water and float; fish that die due to an accident or are freshly dead are not included. The commentary in Urdu reinforces that the prohibition is due to the potential for spoilage.

Key Terms
الطافی (al-taafi): A fish that dies in water and floats to the surface, typically due to natural causes such as illness or injury, and may have started to spoil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Halal and recommended
B) Makruh (disliked)
C) Haram (prohibited)
D) Permissible only if cooked immediately

Key Takeaway
A fish that dies and floats in water is makruh to eat in the Hanafi school, based on the hadith differentiating it from fish cast ashore.

Permissibility of Eel and River-Snake Fish
Ruling statement on two specific fish types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating eel and snake-like fish and explain why snake-like appearance does not affect their permissibility.

Core Idea
All fish are halal regardless of appearance – eel and snake-fish are simply fish.

This short but important passage addresses a common question: are eels and snake-like fish permissible to eat? Despite their unusual appearance, these creatures are biologically fish and fall under the general Hanafi rule that all fish are halal. Let's examine the precise ruling and its basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the creatures
The passage mentions two specific creatures: al-jareeth (eel) and al-mar mahi (river-snake fish). First, confirm what each is: a long, snake-shaped fish.
Al-mar mahi means 'snake-fish' in Persian, common in South Asia.
2
Recognize the ruling phrase
The text uses 'لا بأس' (la ba's), meaning 'there is no harm'. In Hanafi fiqh, this indicates the ruling is mubah (permissible) or halal.
This is a clear permission, not merely tolerated.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The Hanafi school holds that all fish from the sea or river are halal, regardless of shape, scales, or method of death. These creatures are biologically fish, so they are included.
The key is biological classification as fish, not visual similarity to snakes.
4
Address the potential doubt
Some might think snake-like creatures are haram by analogy with land snakes. However, the school distinguishes based on habitat and biology: sea creatures have different rules.
No analogy (qiyas) between sea and land creatures in this context.

Source Text

Commentary
This brief passage rules on two specific types of aquatic creatures: al-jareeth (eel) and al-mar mahi (a snake-like fish commonly called bam machhli in South Asia). The text states clearly that there is no harm (la ba's) in eating them. Although they look like snakes, they are biologically fish and thus fall under the general permissibility of sea creatures. No specific proof is cited here, but the ruling is consistent with the Hanafi position that all fish are halal, and these are simply varieties. The commentary in Urdu adds descriptive details to help identify these creatures.

Key Terms

Permissibility of Locusts Without Slaughter
Ruling with proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on locusts and identify the prophetic proof (dalil) that exempts them from ritual slaughter.

Core Idea
Locusts are halal even dead, like fish — no slaughter required.

This lesson explores the unique ruling on locusts, a permissible insect that does not require the standard dhabh. The Hanafi school relies on explicit hadith to establish this exemption, and the ruling is agreed upon among the schools. Understanding this case clarifies the categories of animals that are halal without slaughter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being ruled upon? The consumption of locusts. The ruling is 'halal' (permissible).
2
Note the specific condition
The text adds 'la dhakata lahu' – no slaughter is required. This is a key exception to the general rule that animals must be slaughtered.
The term 'dhakata' covers both dhabh (throat-slitting) and nahr (spearing for camels).
3
Examine the first dalil
Hadith from Ibn Abi Awfa: The Companions ate locusts in the presence of the Prophet during seven expeditions. This shows tacit approval (sukut al-Shar').
This is a proof of permissibility through practice.
4
Examine the second dalil
Hadith from Ibn Umar: The Prophet explicitly said two dead animals are lawful: fish and locusts. This directly exempts locusts from the need for slaughter.
The hadith is narrated in multiple collections; its meaning is clear (dalala qati?).
5
Understand the epistemological weight
The hadith is considered strong (sahih) and is accepted by all major schools. The Hanafi school classifies it as qati? al-thubut due to widespread acceptance.
There is no ikhtilaf among Hanafis on this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage rules on locusts (al-jarad). The ruling is twofold: first, it is permissible (yajuz) to eat locusts; second, they require no ritual slaughter (la dhakata lahu). The text provides two hadith proofs. The first hadith (from Ibn Abi Awfa) relates that the Companions ate locusts in the presence of the Prophet (peace be upon him) during expeditions, indicating tacit approval. The second hadith (from Ibn Umar) explicitly states that two dead animals are made lawful for the ummah: fish and locusts. Thus, even a locust found dead is halal without the need for slaughter. The commentary in Urdu explains that like fish, locusts are exempt from the normal requirement of dhabh.

Key Terms
الجراد (al-jarad): Locusts, an insect considered halal to eat in Islam without requiring ritual slaughter.
لا ذکاة له (la dhakata lahu): It requires no ritual slaughter (dhabh) to be considered halal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They must be slaughtered by a Muslim
B) They are halal without any slaughter
C) They must be cooked before eating
D) They are halal only if caught alive

Key Takeaway
Locusts are halal to eat without any requirement of ritual slaughter, based on explicit prophetic hadith.

Sharing in Udhiyah: Rulings on the Number of Participants per Animal
Ruling with proofs on participant limits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the maximum number of persons who may share in a camel or cow sacrifice, and explain the evidence for the sheep being restricted to one person.

Core Idea
A sheep is for one person only; a camel or cow is for up to seven — established by explicit prophetic statements.

This passage presents the Hanafi position on how many persons can participate in one sacrificial animal. The ruling is founded on direct hadith evidence, distinguishing between small and large livestock. We will examine the proofs and the contrast with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act being ruled upon (sharing in udhiyah) and the hukm level. The Hanafi ruling: a sheep (shah) is sufficient for one person only; a camel or cow suffices for up to seven persons. Less than seven is allowed.
The hukm level of udhiyah itself is sunnah muakkadah, but this passage addresses the modality of sharing.
2
Locate the dalil for cow/camel
Find the hadith evidence for the maximum of seven. The hadith of Jabir ibn Abdullah: 'A cow is for seven, and a camel is for seven.' (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi).
This is a clear textual proof (dalil) establishing the limit.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah for sheep
How do we know a sheep is for one person only? The hadith of Anas where the Prophet sacrificed two rams, one for himself and one for his household, indicating each ram is for one person. Also, no hadith permits sharing a sheep among multiple individuals.
The absence of permission in transmitted sunnah is itself a proof.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with Shafi'i school
Imam Shafi'i holds that one animal suffices for an entire household, regardless of number, based on the hadith 'Upon every household an udhiyah every year.'
This ikhtilaf arises from different interpretations of the household-based obligation.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school prefers the specific wording of the hadith limiting cow/camel to seven, and the lack of evidence for sharing a sheep. The household hadith is understood as recommending one animal per household, but each adult male should sacrifice individually if capable.
The tarjih is based on the principle that specific (khass) text overrides general (amm) when available.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الشرح الثميرى الجزء الرابع
۳۸
كتاب الاضحية )
شاة او يذبح بدنة او بقرة عن سبعة [ ۲۶۳۰] (۴) وليس على الفقير والمسافر اضحية. بعض اهل العلم لا تجزى الشاة الا ان نفس واحدة وهو قول عبد الله بن المبارك وغيرهم من اهل العلم (الف) ( ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء ان الشاة الواحدة تجرى عن اهل البيت ص ۲۷۶ نمبر ۱۵۰۵) اور ایک بکر اذبح کر کے ثواب میں سب کو شریک کرے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن عائشة ان رسول الله الله امر بكبش اقرن ... وقال بسم الله اللهم تقبل من محمد الله الا الله امر القرن ... ! وآل محمد ومن امة محمد ثم ضحی به (ب) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب ما يستحب من الضحايا ص ۳۰ نمبر ۲۷۹۲) اس حدیث میں ایک بکرے کو ذبح کیا اور اس کا ثواب محمد ، آل محمد اور امت محمد کو پہنچایا۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ ایک جانور اپنی جانب سے ذبح کرے اور اس کا ثواب بہت لوگوں کو پہنچائے یہ جائز ہے۔ اور ان شاء اللہ سب کو پورا پورا ثواب مل جائیگا۔
اور گائے اور اونٹ میں ساتھ آدمی شریک ہو سکتے ہیں اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔

قال انبانا مخنف بن سليم قال ونحن وقوف مع رسول الله الا الله بعرفات قال قال يا ايها الناس ان على كل اهل بيت في كل عام اضحية وعتيرة (د) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب ما جاء فی ایجاب الاضاحی ص ۳۰ نمبر ۲۷۸۸ / ترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء ان الشاة الواحدة سجزی عن اہل البیت ص ۲۷۶ نمبر ۱۵۰۵) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ ایک جانور پورے گھر کی جانب سے کافی ہے چاہے گھر میں کتنے ہی افراد ہوں۔
[2629] (3) And he sacrifices for each one of them, the Sunnah is established upon that (a) (Ibn Majah, chapter on whether sacrifice is obligatory or not, p. 255, no. 3124) From this hadith it is known that sacrifice is Sunnah. [2628] (2) He sacrifices on behalf of himself and on behalf of his minor children... [2629] (3) He slaughters for each one of them a sheep, or he slaughters a camel or a cow on behalf of seven. One sheep is sufficient for one person, not for two. A camel or cow is sufficient for seven persons; more than that cannot share. However, less than seven, like five or four, can share. There is no hadith about sharing a sheep. If sharing a sheep among two people were permissible, it would have been mentioned in hadith. However, it is possible that a person sacrifices a sheep voluntarily and gives its reward to many. From Anas: The Prophet sacrificed two horned rams... (c) (Bukhari, Muslim) This hadith shows that a ram can only be offered for one person. [Page 45] ... A sheep or he slaughters a camel or cow for seven. [2630] (4) Sacrifice is not upon the poor and traveler. Some scholars say a sheep suffices only for one person, which is the saying of Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak and others. (a) (Tirmidhi, no. 1505) And the evidence that one can sacrifice a sheep and share the reward is the hadith: from Aisha that the Prophet commanded a horned ram... and said, 'In the name of Allah, O Allah accept from Muhammad and the family of Muhammad and the ummah of Muhammad,' then he sacrificed it. (b) (Abu Dawud, no. 2792) From this hadith it is known that one can sacrifice an animal on his own behalf and give its reward to many. And for cow and camel, seven can share, the evidence is the hadith of Jabir: the Prophet said, 'A cow is for seven, and a camel is for seven' (c) (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi). Now Imam Shafi'i says that if there are twelve people in one household, one animal suffices for all of them. However, one animal does not suffice for two households. According to him, one animal is required for one household, regardless of how many members. (d) (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi: hadith about sacrifice and atirah).
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proofs about the number of persons for whom one animal suffices in udhiyah. The author states that a sheep (shah) is sufficient for one person only, and a camel or cow is sufficient for up to seven persons. The proof for the sheep is the hadith of Anas that the Prophet sacrificed two rams, one for himself and one for his household, indicating that each sheep is for one person. For cow and camel, the hadith of Jabir explicitly states 'a cow is for seven and a camel is for seven.' The passage also discusses sharing reward: one can sacrifice a sheep and dedicate its reward to many, based on the hadith where the Prophet sacrificed a ram and included his family and ummah in the supplication. Note that Imam Shafi'i holds that one animal suffices for an entire household, regardless of number, based on the hadith 'upon every household an udhiyah every year.' The Hanafi view, however, restricts sharing in large animals (cow/camel) to seven, and does not allow sharing a sheep among multiple persons, though reward can be shared.

Key Terms
جزور (jazur): A camel (specifically a male or female camel suitable for sacrifice).
شاة (shah): A sheep or goat suitable for sacrifice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this text, how many persons may share in the sacrifice of one cow?
A) 1
B) 5
C) 7
D) Unlimited

Key Takeaway
A sheep is for one person only; a camel or cow can be shared by up to seven persons, as established by explicit prophetic statements.

Ruling: Sacrifice Not Required for Poor or Traveler
Ruling on exemption from udhiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on udhiyah for the poor and traveler and identify the hadith proof that conditions it on financial capacity.

Core Idea
Sacrifice is only required for those with capacity; the poor and traveler are exempt.

This lesson clarifies that the Sunnah mu'akkadah of udhiyah is conditioned upon having financial capacity (sa'ah). The passage cites a hadith from Abu Huraira to establish that sacrifice is not incumbent on the poor or the traveler, contrary to the views of some other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is udhiyah (sacrifice). The ruling is that it is not upon the poor or traveler. Understand that it is sunnah mu'akkadah, not wajib.
The Hanafi school considers udhiyah sunnah mu'akkadah, but only for those with capacity.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Abu Huraira: 'Whoever has capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not come near our prayer place.'
Ibn Majah, no. 3123.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'man kana lahu sa'ah' conditions the obligation/recommendation on having capacity. The poor and traveler lack this capacity, so they are exempt.
'Sa'ah' means financial ability beyond basic needs.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider udhiyah wajib on every adult Muslim who has the means, including travelers. The Hanafi school disagrees based on the same hadith.
The difference is in interpretation of the threat in the hadith and usul principles.
5
Evaluate the practice
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is that sacrifice is not required from the poor or traveler. A poor person who sacrifices is valid and rewarded, but not obliged.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ ۲۶۳۰] (۴) وليس على الفقير والمسافر اضحية. اوپر حدیث گزری کہ جو گنجائش رکھتا ہو یعنی صاحب نصاب ہو اس پر قربانی ہے اس لئے فقیر پر قربانی نہیں ہے۔ فقیر کے پاس کچھ ہے ہی نہیں تو وہ کیسے قربانی کرے گا (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابي هريرة أن رسول الله الله قال من كان له سعة ولم يضح فلا يقربن مصلانا (ه) (ابن ماجه شریف، باب الاضاحی واجہ بھی ام لا ؟ ص ۴۵۵ نمبر ۳۱۲۳) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ گنجائش ہو پھر اس پر قربانی ہے ورنہ نہیں۔
[2630] (4) Sacrifice is not upon the poor and the traveler. The hadith passed that whoever has capacity, i.e., is sahib nisab, then sacrifice is upon him; therefore, it is not upon the poor. The poor have nothing, so how will they sacrifice? (2) There is a hadith: from Abu Huraira that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever has capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not come near our prayer place.' (h) (Ibn Majah, chapter on whether sacrifice is obligatory or not, p. 455, no. 3123) From this hadith it is known that if one has capacity, then sacrifice is upon him; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a ruling stating that udhiyah is not obligatory upon the poor or the traveler. The author reasons that the previous hadith indicated that sacrifice is for those who have capacity (sahib nisab), so the poor are excluded. The proof is the hadith of Abu Huraira: 'Whoever has capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not come near our prayer place.' This hadith emphasizes that sacrifice is required from those who have the means, thus it is not required from those who do not. The phrase 'sa'ah' (capacity) refers to financial ability to purchase an animal beyond one's basic needs. The traveler is also exempt because he is not settled and may not have the means readily available.

Key Terms
سعة (sa'ah): Financial capacity beyond basic needs, i.e., being sahib nisab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who is exempt from offering the udhiyah sacrifice?
A) Only the poor
B) The poor and the traveler
C) Only the traveler
D) All Muslims

Key Takeaway
Udhiyah is not obligatory upon the poor or traveler because the condition of financial capacity (sa'ah) is not met, as proven by the hadith of Abu Huraira.

Permissible Sacrifice: Animals with Non-Major Defects
Ruling on permissibility of animals with non-major defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four non-major defects that do not invalidate a sacrificial animal in the Hanafi school and apply the ruling to identify permissible animals.

Core Idea
Hornless, castrated, scabby, and slightly mad animals are valid for sacrifice—these are not defects.

The Hanafi school permits sacrificing animals that have certain conditions which some may consider defects. This lesson clarifies which four conditions are not considered impediments to validity, supported by the Prophet's own practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on the permissibility of sacrifice (udhiyah) for specific animals. The hukm is that it is permissible (ja'iz).
2
Name the four animals and their conditions
The text mentions: al-jamma' (hornless), al-khasi (castrated), al-jarba' (scabby/mangy), and al-thawla' (slightly mad). Understand the Arabic terms.
3
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah that the Prophet sacrificed two castrated rams. This is a Sunnah proof.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's explicit act of sacrificing castrated rams indicates permissibility. The word 'مجئين' confirms castration.
5
Note the ikhtilaf and boundaries
Some scholars may consider castration a defect, but Hanafis hold it permissible. The commentary adds conditions: severe madness or deep scabs invalidate.

This passage is severely corrupted and cannot be translated properly due to OCR errors. The original lesson should contain the ruling: It is permissible to sacrifice a hornless (jamma'), castrated (khasi'), scabby (jarba'), and slightly mad (thawla') animal. The commentary provides hadith proofs, including the hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah that the Prophet sacrificed two horned, piebald, castrated rams. It also discusses limitations such as severe madness or deep scabs.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This lesson states that animals with certain non-major defects are permissible for sacrifice. The defects mentioned are: being hornless (jamma'), meaning born without horns; castrated (khasi'), which is recommended due to better meat; scabby (jarba'), as long as it is still healthy and fat; and slightly mad (thawla'), as long as it can still eat. These are not considered major defects. The hadith from Jabir confirms that the Prophet sacrificed castrated rams, demonstrating its permissibility. However, the commentary adds that if the madness is severe or the scab penetrates the flesh, it becomes impermissible.

Key Terms
الجماء (al-jamma'): An animal born without horns.
الخصى (al-khasi): A castrated animal.
الجرباء (al-jarba'): An animal with scabies or mange, as long as it is not severe.
الثولاء (al-thawla'): An animal with mild mental disturbance (slightly mad), still able to eat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a permissible condition for a sacrificial animal?
A) An animal born without horns.
B) An animal with a broken horn that has lost more than half.
C) An animal that is blind in both eyes.
D) An animal with a deep scab that spoils its meat.

Key Takeaway
Animals with non-major defects (hornlessness, castration, superficial scab, mild madness) are valid for sacrifice in the Hanafi school.

Other Lessons
beginner
6 min
Permissible Species for Udhiyah
Ruling on permissible species for udhiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three permissible species for udhiyah and identify whether a given animal is valid for sacrifice in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Only camels, cattle, and sheep/goats are valid for sacrifice; buffalo is included by analogy.

This lesson presents the foundational rule for the sacrificial animal (udhiyah) in the Hanafi school. The matn (text) specifies that the sacrifice is confined to three categories of domesticated livestock. Understanding these species is crucial because any other animal, even if halal to eat, does not fulfill the requirement of udhiyah. The Hanafi school also includes buffalo by clear analogy (qiyas jaliy) and requires the animal to be domesticated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is udhiyah? It is the animal sacrificed on Eid al-Adha. This passage gives the ruling on which species are valid. The Hanafi school considers udhiyah wajib for those who have the means.
The obligation of udhiyah is established elsewhere; here we focus only on the permissible animal types.
2
Memorize the three permitted species
The matn lists three categories: al-ibil (camels), al-baqar (cattle), and al-ghanam (sheep and goats). These are the only valid types.
Al-ghanam includes both sheep and goats.
3
Apply the exclusion rule
Any animal not from these three species is invalid for udhiyah, even if it is halal to eat (e.g., deer, chicken). Additionally, the animal must be domesticated; wild animals are excluded.
The commentary adds that buffalo is included by qiyas because it resembles cattle in all relevant aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۶۳۶] (١٠) والاضحية من الابل والبقر والغنم
[2636] (10) The sacrificial animal is from camels, cattle, and sheep/goats.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This is a concise ruling (matn) stating the species permissible for udhiyah. The three categories are: camels (ibil), cattle (baqar, including buffalo by analogy), and sheep/goats (ghanam, which includes both sheep and goats). The commentary clarifies that only domesticated animals are valid; wild animals like deer, even if raised at home, are not permissible because they are not from these species. This ruling is based on the practice of the Prophet and the consensus of the scholars. Buffalo is included through qiyas because it resembles cattle in all relevant legal aspects.

Key Terms
الابل (al-ibil): Camels, including both male and female, but must be of the domesticated kind.
البقر (al-baqar): Cattle, including cows and bulls; by qiyas, buffalo is included.
الغنم (al-ghanam): Sheep and goats collectively; both are valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following animals is NOT valid for qurbani according to the Hanafi school?
A) A cow
B) A goat
C) A deer that has been domesticated
D) A buffalo

Key Takeaway
The sacrifice is only valid from camels, cattle, and sheep/goats; no other animal qualifies.

Permissible Minor Defects in Sacrificial Animals
Ruling on permissible non-serious defects in sacrifice animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which minor defects do not invalidate a sacrifice animal and apply the Hanafi criterion distinguishing permissible from impermissible defects.

Core Idea
A sacrifice animal is valid if sound in meat — minor congenital or cosmetic defects are not bars.

This passage from al-Sharh al-Saghir addresses a practical question: which animals with apparent flaws are still acceptable for udhiyyah? The author lists four categories that are permissible, contrasting them with genuine defects like a broken horn or severed ear. Understanding this distinction helps avoid unnecessary rejection of valid animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The text begins with 'ويجوز' (it is permissible), establishing the hukm for four specific conditions. Recognize this is a ruling on the validity of the sacrifice (udhiyyah), not a general recommendation.
2
List the four permissible conditions
Extract the four terms: الجماء (born without horns), الخصى (castrated), الجرباء (minor mange), الثولاء (slight madness). Note each is described as permissible despite the apparent defect.
These are considered non-defects in the Hanafi school as long as the meat quality is unaffected.
3
Identify the underlying criterion
The common thread is that the animal is 'sound in meat' (لحمها سليم). The ruling excludes defects that significantly impair the meat or are explicitly prohibited (e.g., more than half horn broken, ear cut).
The core principle: defects that reduce meat quantity or quality invalidate; cosmetic or minor variations do not.
4
Contrast with impermissible defects
The text mentions الاعضب (more than half horn broken) as prohibited, citing the athar from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab. Also, from the hadith reference, an ear-cut animal is not permissible. Understand the threshold: less than half horn broken might still be permissible? The text does not address precisely, but the principle is that significant impairment invalidates.
The source distinguishes between congenital absence of horns (permissible) and traumatic breakage of horns (may be defective).
5
Analyse the dalil for castration
The hadith of Jabir (رضي الله عنه) describes the Prophet ﷺ sacrificing two castrated rams. This demonstrates permissibility (ja'iz) and even preferability (better meat). Recognize this as a sunnah ahad proof with zanni thubut but qati dalala in this context.
The proof is direct action of the Prophet ﷺ, indicating not only permissibility but also desirability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹) ويجوز ان يُضحى بالجماء والخصى والجرباء والثولاء [۲۶۳۶] (١٠) والاضحية من الابل والبقر والغنم ما یکرہ من الضحايا ص ۳۱ نمبر ۲۸۰۴/۲۸۰۳ / ترمذی شریف، باب ما یکرہ من الضاحی ص ۲۷۵ نمبر ۱۴۹۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ کان کٹا ہوا ہو تو جائز نہیں ہے۔ اور کٹنے کے بارے میں حضرت سعید سے مروی ہے کہ آدھا سے زیادہ ہوا تو جائز نہیں ہے۔ اثر یہ ہے۔ قلت یعنی لسعيد بن المسيب ما الاعضب ؟ قال النصف فما فوقه (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب ما يكره من الضحا یا ص ۱۳۱ نمبر ۲۸۰۶ سفن للبیہقی نمبر ۱۹۱۰۴) [۲۶۳۵] (۹) اور جائز ہے کہ قربانی کی جائے بے سینگ والے کی اور خصی کی اور کھجلی والے کی اور دیوانے کی۔ جانور گوشت کے اعتبار سے ٹھیک ٹھاک ہو لیکن پیدائشی طور پر سینگ ہو ہی نہیں۔ یہ بات نہیں ہے کہ سینگ تھا لیکن آدھے سے زیادہ ٹوٹ گیا ہو تو ایسے جانور کی قربانی جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ عیب نہیں ہے، بہت سے جانور کو پیدائشی طور پر سینگ ہوتا ہی نہیں ہے۔ اور جائز ہے خصی کی قربانی یا کھجلی والا جانور ہے لیکن موٹا تازہ ہے تو جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ تھوڑا بہت کھجلی ہونا کوئی بڑا عیب نہیں ہے۔ اسی طرح جانور دیوانہ ہولیکن گوشت کے اعتبار سے ٹھیک ٹھاک ہو تو جائز ہے۔ یہ اس قسم کے عیب نہیں ہیں کہ قربانی جائز نہ ہو۔ خصی جائز ہونے کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عـن جـابـر بـن عبـد الله قال ذبح النبي يوم الذبح كبشين اقرنين املحين مجئين (ب) ( ابوداؤد شریف، باب ما يستحب من الضحايا ص ۳۰ نمبر ۲۷۹۵ رابن ماجه شریف، باب اضاحی رسول الله ص ۲۵۵ نمبر۳۱۲۲) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ خصی کیا ہوا جانور قربانی میں جائز ہے بلکہ اس کا گوشت اچھا ہوتا ہے اس لئے اور بھی بہتر ہے۔
It is permissible to sacrifice an animal without horns, a castrated one, a mangy one, and a mad one. ... From this hadith it is known that an ear-cut animal is not permissible. Regarding cutting, it is narrated from Sa'id that if more than half is cut, it is not permissible. The effect is: I said to Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab: What is a'dab? He said: Half or more. ... It is permissible to sacrifice an animal without horns, castrated, mangy, and mad. The animal may be sound in meat but born without horns. This is not like the horn that is more than half broken; such an animal's sacrifice is permitted because it is not a defect. Many animals are born without horns. And it is permissible to sacrifice a castrated animal or a mangy animal if it is fat and fresh, because a little manginess is not a major defect. Similarly, an animal that is mad but sound in meat is permissible. These are not defects that prevent sacrifice. The proof for castration being permissible is the hadith: Jabir ibn Abdullah said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) sacrificed on the day of sacrifice two castrated, horned, white-and-black rams. This hadith shows that castrated animals are permissible in sacrifice, and their meat is better.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage clarifies that certain minor defects do not invalidate an animal for sacrifice (udhiyyah). The author lists four types: (1) al-jamma' – an animal born without horns; this is not a defect as it does not affect meat quality. However, if the horn is broken beyond half, that is a defect (al-a'dab) and such an animal is not permissible. (2) al-khasi – a castrated animal; the author cites the hadith that the Prophet himself sacrificed castrated rams, which is a strong proof. (3) al-jarba' – an animal with minor mange that does not affect the meat; if mange is severe and penetrates the meat, it becomes defective. (4) al-thawla' – an animal slightly mad but still able to eat and function. The key principle is that defects that significantly impair the meat or the animal's health are prohibited, while minor or congenital variations are permissible.

Key Terms
الجماء (al-jamma'): An animal born without horns (congenital).
الخصى (al-khasi): A castrated animal (male).
الجرباء (al-jarba'): An animal with minor mange (skin disease) that does not affect the meat.
الثولاء (al-thawla'): An animal slightly mad but still able to eat and is sound in meat.
الاعضب (al-a'dab): An animal with more than half of its horn broken off.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following animals is NOT permissible for sacrifice according to this passage?
A) An animal born without horns (al-jamma')
B) A castrated animal (al-khasi)
C) An animal with more than half of its horn broken (al-a'dab)
D) An animal with minor mange (al-jarba')

Key Takeaway
An animal for sacrifice is valid if it is sound in meat; minor or congenital defects such as being hornless, castrated, slightly mangy, or slightly mad do not invalidate the sacrifice.

Eligible Animal Types for Udhiyyah
Listing of valid animal species for sacrifice with qiyas for buffalo

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three primary species and explain the inclusion of buffalo via qiyas, and identify the condition of domestication.

Core Idea
Only domesticated camels, cows, goats, and buffalo (by analogy) are valid for sacrifice.

This passage specifies the livestock eligible for udhiyyah. The Hanafi school, based on hadith and qiyas, restricts sacrifice to specific domesticated species. Understanding these categories ensures that the sacrifice fulfills the requirements of the sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
The passage begins by stating that the sacrifice (udhiyyah) is from camels, cows, and goats. These are the core species mentioned in hadith.
Note the Arabic terms: الابل (camels), البقر (cows), الغنم (sheep/goats).
2
Note the qiyas extension
The commentator adds that buffalo is also valid by analogy (qiyas) to cows because both fall under domesticated cattle.
Hanafi school uses qiyas to extend the ruling to buffalo, while other schools may differ.
3
Recognize the domestication condition
The passage excludes animals that are not originally domesticated, even if tamed, such as deer. Only an'am (domesticated livestock) are acceptable.
The condition is based on the species, not individual upbringing.
4
Apply the ruling to examples
Test your understanding: a camel is valid, a buffalo is valid, a domesticated deer is not valid.
This step consolidates the two key conditions: species and domestication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage specifies the types of livestock eligible for udhiyyah. The primary categories are camels, cows, and goats (which include sheep and goats). The underlying principle is that only domesticated animals (an‘am) are valid; wild animals like deer, even if tamed and raised domestically, do not qualify because the texts refer specifically to domesticated livestock.

Key Terms
