کتاب الایمان
۲۶۴۷] (۳) فهذه اليمين ياثم بها صاحبها ولا كفارة فيها الا التوبة والاستغفار [۲۶۴۸] (۴) واليمين المنعقدة هى ان يحلف على الامر المستقبل ان يفعله او لا يفعله.

جانتے ہوئے کہ ایسا نہیں ہوا یا نہیں کیا اس پر قسم کھانا یمین غموس ہے۔
[۲۶۴۶] (۳) پس اس قسم سے گنہ گار ہو گا اس کا کرنے والا اور اس میں کفارہ نہیں ہے سوائے تو بہ اور استغفار کے۔
سیمین غموس میں کفارہ نہیں ہے صرف تو بہ اور استغفار ہے۔ او پر اثر میں گزرا کہ یمین غموس میں کفارہ نہیں ہے۔ عن ابراهيم ... والله لقد فعلت ليس في شيء منه كفارة إن كان تعمد شيئا فهو كذب (سن لليبقى نمبر ۱۹۸۸۲) (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ قال ابن مسعود کنا نعد من الذنب الذى لا كفارة له اليمين الغموس فقيل ما اليمين الغموس ؟ قال اقتطاع الرجل مال اخيه باليمين الكاذبة (الف) (سنن مهم ن للبیہقی ، باب ما جاء فی الیمین الغموس ج عاشر ص ۶۷ نمبر ۱۹۸۸۳ مستدرک حاکم، کتاب الایمان والنذ ورج رابع ص ۳۲۹ نمبر ۷۸۰۹ ) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ یمین غموس
میں کفارہ نہیں ہے۔
امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ یمین غموس میں بھی کفارہ ہے۔
ان کی دلیل اس حدیث کا اشارہ ہے۔ عن ابی بردة عن ابيه . وانى والله ان شاء الله لا احلف على يمين فاري غيرها خيرا منها الا كفرت عن يميني واتيت الذى هو خير (ب) ( بخاری شریف ، باب قول الله تعالی لا یواخذكم الله باللغوص ۹۸۰ نمبر ۶۶۲۳ مسلم شریف ، باب ندب من حلف یمینا فرای غیر ها خیر امتصاص ۴۶ نمبر ۱۶۴۹) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ کسی چیز پر تم کھاؤں اور دیکھوں کہ وہ چیز اچھی نہیں ہے تو کفارہ دے کر اس کے خلاف کردوں اور جھوٹ اچھی نہیں ہے اس لئے اس کے خلاف کر کے کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔
افت یا تم : گنہگار ہوگا۔
As for yamin ghamus, it is swearing about a past matter intentionally lying. Regarding [the case] where one swears saying 'I will do such and such,' then by that swearing he makes a matter binding. If he cannot do that act, then kaffarah is obligatory upon him: either free a slave, or feed ten poor people, or clothe ten poor people with average clothing, or fast three days. Allah mentions this in the above verse. In this hadith: from Abu Burda from his father said: I came to the Prophet ﷺ in a group of Ash'aris... and indeed, by Allah, if I swear an oath and then see something better than it, I will expiate my oath and do what is better, or do what is better and expiate my oath (Bukhari, Muslim). From this hadith it is understood that if it is a binding oath, then breaking it requires kaffarah. As for yamin laghw, it is to swear about a past matter that it happened, believing it to have happened, but it did not happen. This is called yamin laghw; there is no kaffarah for it. The above verse mentions it: 'Allah will not take you to account for the unintentional in your oaths, but He will take you to account for what you have contracted in oaths' (5:89). There is an athar: I heard al-Sha'bi saying: Righteousness and sin are based on what one swears according to his knowledge, thinking it to be so; there is no sin and no kaffarah upon him (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq). From this athar it is understood that there is no kaffarah in yamin laghw. [2645] (2) So yamin ghamus is: swearing about a past matter knowingly lying. He knows that the matter is not so, yet knowingly lies and says it is so. This is called yamin ghamus. Another athar: He said: Then yamin ghamus. I said to 'Amir: What is yamin ghamus? He said: The one who seizes the property of a Muslim by his oath while he is lying in it (Sunan al-Bayhaqi). From this athar it is understood that taking someone's property by lying is called yamin ghamus. And in another athar: From Ibrahim he said: Oaths are four: two oaths require expiation, and two oaths do not require expiation: saying 'By Allah, I did not do it' or 'By Allah, I did it' — there is no kaffarah in any of it; if he deliberately does something, it is a lie, and if he thinks it is as he said, it is laghw (Sunan al-Bayhaqi). From this athar it is understood that [PAGE_056] (49 Kitab al-Iman) [2647] (3) So this oath, its doer sins, and there is no kaffarah in it except repentance and seeking forgiveness. [2648] (4) And the binding oath (yamin mun'aqidah) is to swear about a future matter to do it or not to do it. Knowing that it did not happen or he did not do it, swearing on it is yamin ghamus. [2646] (3) So with this oath, the doer becomes sinful, and there is no kaffarah in it except tawbah and istighfar. In yamin ghamus there is no kaffarah, only tawbah and istighfar. The previous athar passed that there is no kaffarah in yamin ghamus: from Ibrahim... 'By Allah, I did it' — there is no kaffarah in any of it; if he deliberately does something, it is a lie (Sunan al-Bayhaqi). And in another athar: Ibn Mas'ud said: We used to count among sins for which there is no kaffarah the yamin ghamus. It was said: What is yamin ghamus? He said: A man seizing his brother's property by a false oath (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Mustadrak al-Hakim). From this athar it is understood that there is no kaffarah in yamin ghamus. Imam al-Shafi'i says that in yamin ghamus there is also kaffarah. His proof is an indication from the hadith: from Abu Burda from his father: 'And by Allah, if I swear an oath and see something better than it, I will expiate my oath and do what is better' (Bukhari, Muslim). In this hadith, if one swears about something and sees that it is not good, then he gives kaffarah and does the opposite. And lying is not good, so doing the opposite requires kaffarah. So the ruling: he becomes sinful.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal ruling of yamin ghamus (a false oath about a past event). The author first defines yamin ghamus as swearing knowingly lying about a past matter. He then presents the Hanafi position: yamin ghamus is a major sin but does not require kaffarah; only sincere repentance (tawbah) and seeking forgiveness (istighfar) are required. The evidence includes athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) explicitly stating that no kaffarah applies. The author then presents the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that kaffarah is required in yamin ghamus as well, citing the general hadith about expiating oaths when breaking them. The Hanafi response, implied by the structure, is that the hadith refers to binding oaths about future actions, not false oaths about the past. This is a classic example where the Hanafi school distinguishes between oaths based on their subject matter and intent, using istihsan and specific athar to limit the general command of kaffarah. The passage also clarifies the definition of yamin laghw (vain oath) to contrast with yamin ghamus.

Key Terms
الیمین الغموس (al-yamin al-ghamus): An oath about a past event that is deliberately false; the swearer knows the truth but lies. It is called 'ghamus' because it immerses the swearer in sin.
الیمین اللغو (al-yamin al-laghw): An oath about a past event that the swearer believes to be true but is actually false; there is no deliberate lie. No sin and no kaffarah.
الیمین المنعقدة (al-yamin al-mun'aqidah): A binding oath about a future action, resolved to do or not do something. Breaking it requires kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the required action for a person who commits yamin ghamus?
A) He must free a slave, feed ten poor, or fast three days.
B) He must only repent and seek forgiveness, with no financial expiation.
C) He must give charity equal to the value of what he lied about.
D) He must repeat the oath truthfully to nullify it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that yamin ghamus requires no kaffarah, only repentance; this is based on explicit statements of the Salaf and the Quranic distinction between vain and contracted oaths.

Definition and Ruling of Yamin Mun'aqidah
Defines yamin mun'aqidah and states its ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define yamin mun'aqidah, state its ruling, and identify the conditions that make an oath binding.

Core Idea
A binding oath about a future action must be fulfilled; breaking it requires kaffarah.

This lesson introduces yamin mun'aqidah, the binding oath concerning a future action. Understanding this type of oath is essential because it carries legal consequences: if broken deliberately, kaffarah (expiation) becomes obligatory. The passage defines the term, provides an example, and cites a prophetic hadith to illustrate its usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
Yamin mun'aqidah is an oath about a future action, either to do it or not do it. Note that it is deliberate and made with resolve.
2
State the Ruling
Such an oath is binding (mun'aqidah), meaning fulfilling it is wajib (obligatory) as long as the action is permissible and possible.
The ruling is derived from the Qur'an and Sunnah; breaking it without a valid excuse is haram.
3
Understand the Consequences
If the oath is deliberately broken, kaffarah becomes wajib: freeing a slave, feeding/clothing ten poor, or fasting three days.
The obligation of kaffarah is established by Qur'an 5:89.
4
Recognize the Conditions
The swearer must be a Muslim, sane, and mature. The action sworn must be possible and not intrinsically sinful.
If the action is sinful or impossible, the oath is not binding and no kaffarah is required.
5
Apply to the Hadith
The Prophet (ﷺ) swore 'By Allah, I cannot mount you' – this is an example of a binding oath about a future action (not providing mounts). It shows that even the Prophet used such oaths, confirming their legitimacy.
The hadith is from Bukhari, reported by Abu Burda.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واليمين المنعقدة هي ان يحلف على الامر المستقبل ان يفعله او لا يفعله. مثل قسم کھائےکہ خدا کی تم میں ضرور دعوت کروں گا یا خداکی قسم میں دعوت ہرگز نہیں کروں گا۔ اس طرح آئندہ کے بارے میں کسی کام کے کرنے یا نہ کرنے کے بارے میں قسم کھائے اس کو یمین منعقدہ کہتے ہیں۔ سواری کے لئے اونٹ دینے کے بارے میں آپ نے قسم کھائی۔ عن ابی بردۃ قال اتيت النبي الله في رهط من الاشعريين استحمله فقال والله لا احملكم وما عندى ما احملكم عليه (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب قول الله تعالی لا یواخذ كم الله باللغو ص ۹۸۰ نمبر ۶۶۲۳ )
And the binding oath (yamin mun'aqidah) is that one swears about a future matter to do it or not do it. For example, one swears 'By Allah, I will definitely invite you' or 'By Allah, I will never invite.' In this way, swearing about doing or not doing a future act is called yamin mun'aqidah. Regarding giving a camel for riding, the Prophet swore an oath: from Abu Burda he said: I came to the Prophet ﷺ in a group of Ash'aris asking for mounts, so he said: 'By Allah, I cannot mount you, for I have nothing to mount you on.' (Bukhari, narrated by Abu Burda)
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines yamin mun'aqidah (the binding oath), which is an oath concerning a future action — either to do it or not to do it. The author gives an example: swearing to invite someone or not to invite. The key point is that this type of oath is binding (mun'aqidah) in the sense that if the swearer deliberately breaks it, kaffarah becomes obligatory. The author cites the hadith of Abu Burda where the Prophet swore by Allah that he could not provide mounts, illustrating that even the Prophet used binding oaths. Although the ruling of kaffarah is not explicitly stated in this short passage, from the context (the previous page discussed kaffarah for breaking such oaths) it is understood that breaking a yamin mun'aqidah requires kaffarah (freeing a slave, feeding/clothing ten poor, or fasting three days). This is a straightforward definition-ruling passage meant to distinguish this category from yamin laghw and yamin ghamus.

Key Terms
الیمین المنعقدة (al-yamin al-mun'aqidah): An oath concerning a future action, made with resolve, that becomes binding; breaking it requires kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of oath requires kaffarah when broken?
A) Yamin laghw
B) Yamin mun'aqidah
C) Yamin ghamus
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
A deliberate oath about a future permissible action is binding; breaking it necessitates kaffarah as expiation.

The Three Types of Oaths in Hanafi Fiqh: Ghamus, Mun'aqida, and Laghw
Defines three oath types and contrasts Hanafi and Shafi'i on Ghamus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of oaths (ghamus, mun'aqida, laghw) and state the Hanafi ruling for each, including the Shafi'i objection on yamin ghamus.

Core Idea
A false oath about the past (ghamus) is a major sin with no expiation; only repentance suffices.

Oaths are serious matters in Islamic law, carrying legal and spiritual consequences. Not all oaths are treated equally. The Hanafi school classifies oaths based on timeframe and intent. This lesson unpacks the three categories—Yamin Ghamus, Yamin Mun'aqida, and Yamin Laghw—and explores a key disagreement between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the false oath of the past.

Guided Path
1
Classify by Timeframe and Intent
Oaths refer to either past or future events. For past events, the swearer either knows the truth or is mistaken. For future events, the oath creates a binding commitment. Identify which type each classification (Ghamus, Mun'aqida, Laghw) falls into.
Ghamus and Laghw both deal with past events; the key difference is knowledge vs. mistake.
2
Examine the Hanafi Ruling on Yamin Ghamus
In Hanafi fiqh, swearing falsely about the past with full knowledge is a major sin (ithm) but does not require expiation (kaffarah). The only remedy is tawbah and istighfar. Note the proofs: statements of Ibn Mas'ud and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.
This is the point of disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i.
3
Analyze Yamin Mun'aqida and Its Consequence
A binding oath about the future, if broken (hanith), obligates kaffarah as per Quran 5:89. Identify the details of kaffarah (feeding/clothing/fasting). Understand that the hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) about expiating oaths applies here, not to Ghamus.
The same hadith used by Shafi'is for Ghamus is interpreted by Hanafis as specific to future oaths.
4
Understand Yamin Laghw and Its Ruling
When someone swears about a past event believing it to be true, but it is false, there is no sin and no kaffarah. This is supported by Quran 5:89 and the athar of al-Sha'bi. Note the difference from Ghamus: ignorance versus knowledge.
This category is called 'unintentional' or 'vain' oath.
5
Evaluate the Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that Yamin Ghamus does require kaffarah, based on the general command in the hadith to expiate any oath when a better alternative appears. The Hanafi response: the hadith refers to binding oaths (Mun'aqida), not falsehood about the past. Deliberate lying requires repentance, not expiation.
This dispute has practical consequences for a person who committed a false oath.

Source Text

Book of Oaths
(3) So this oath - the one who swears it sins, and there is no expiation for it except repentance and seeking forgiveness.
Knowing that such did not happen or was not done, swearing about it is the sinful oath (Yamin Ghamus).
So the one who does this oath is sinful, and there is no expiation for it except repentance and seeking forgiveness.
There is no expiation in Yamin Ghamus; only repentance and seeking forgiveness. An athar (narration from a Companion) has passed that there is no expiation in Yamin Ghamus: from Ibrahim... 'By Allah, I have done something' there is no expiation in any of it; if he deliberately did something, it is a lie. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi no. 19882). Also an athar: Ibn Mas'ud said, 'We used to consider among the sins that have no expiation the sinful oath.' It was asked, 'What is the sinful oath?' He said, 'That a man appropriates the property of his brother by means of a false oath.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab ma ja'a fi al-yamin al-ghamus, vol. 10 p. 67 no. 19883; Mustadrak al-Hakim, Kitab al-Iman wa al-Nudhur, vol. 4 p. 329 no. 7809). From this athar it becomes known that there is no expiation in Yamin Ghamus.
Imam al-Shafi'i says that there is expiation in Yamin Ghamus as well.
Their evidence is the indication of this hadith: from Abu Burda from his father: 'And verily, by Allah, if Allah wills, I do not swear an oath and then see something better than it except that I expiate my oath and do that which is better.' (Bukhari, Bab qawl Allah ta'ala la yu'akhidhukum Allah bil-laghwi, no. 6623; Muslim, Bab nadbi man halafa yaminan fa-ra'a ghayraha khayran minha, no. 1649). In this hadith: if you swear on something and see that thing is not good, then give expiation and do the opposite, and lying is not good, so doing the opposite would necessitate expiation.
Fatwa: He is sinful.
(4) And the binding oath (Yamin Mun'aqida) is that one swears about a future matter: that he will do it or will not do it.
For example, he swears: By Allah, I will definitely invite you, or By Allah, I will never invite. In this manner, swearing about doing or not doing something in the future is called Yamin Mun'aqida. Regarding giving a camel for riding, the Prophet (peace be upon him) swore: from Abu Burda said: I came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) with a group of Ash'aris asking for mounts; he said, 'By Allah, I cannot carry you, and I have nothing with which to carry you.' (Bukhari, Bab qawl Allah ta'ala la yu'akhidhukum Allah bil-laghwi). Since he swore about a future action (not giving mounts), this is called Yamin Mun'aqida.
(5) Then when he breaks that oath (hanith), expiation becomes incumbent upon him.
The verse has passed: 'Allah will not call you to account for the unintentional in your oaths, but He will call you to account for what you have contracted in oaths. Its expiation is the feeding of ten poor persons from the average of what you feed your own families, or clothing them, or freeing a slave. But whoever cannot find [the means] then a fast of three days. That is the expiation of your oaths when you have sworn.' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:89). This verse mentions that when one breaks a binding oath, expiation is due. Then the details of expiation are given. A hadith also passed: 'I do not swear an oath and then see something better than it except that I expiate my oath and do that which is better.' (Bukhari no. 6623, Muslim no. 1649). In this hadith: if I break the oath, I give expiation.
(6) And the vain oath (Yamin Laghw) is that one swears about past matters while assuming that it is as he says, whereas the matter is contrary to it. This is an oath; we hope that Allah will not take the swearer to account for it.
Regarding a past event, the swearer assumed that the matter was so, and swore on that, while the matter was contrary. This is Yamin Laghw. It is hoped that Allah will not take him to account. The verse says: 'Allah will not call you to account for the unintentional in your oaths.' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:89). This verse indicates that Allah does not take to account for Yamin Laghw. For the details of Yamin Laghw, there is this athar: I heard al-Sha'bi say: 'Righteousness and sin are based on what one swears according to his knowledge while he thinks it is so; there is no sin in it and no expiation upon him.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-laghwi wa ma huwa? vol. 8 p. 475 no. 15957; Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab ma ja'a fi al-yamin al-ghamus, vol. 10 p. 67 no. 19882). This athar says that swearing while assuming according to one's knowledge is Yamin Laghw. Another narration says that swearing idly by Allah (without deliberation) is called Yamin Laghw; its evidence is the hadith: Aisha said: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him)...'
(Footnote continued from previous page): ...
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage from Al-Sharh al-Thamiri presents a comprehensive classification of oaths in Hanafi fiqh. The author defines three types: (1) Yamin Ghamus (sinful/false oath about the past), (2) Yamin Mun'aqida (binding oath about the future), and (3) Yamin Laghw (vain oath about the past).

The author begins with Yamin Ghamus: when someone knowingly swears falsely about a past event, this is a major sin (ithm) but has no expiation (kaffarah). The only remedy is sincere repentance (tawbah) and seeking forgiveness (istighfar). Proofs are provided from the statements of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and Ibn Mas'ud (radiallahu anhu), who both affirm that such an oath carries no kaffarah. The author then presents the Shafi'i position: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that Yamin Ghamus does require kaffarah, based on the general hadith about expiating any oath when a better alternative is seen. The Hanafi response implicitly is that this hadith applies to future-binding oaths, not to false oaths about the past, as the latter are sins of lying and cannot be undone by kaffarah.

The second type is Yamin Mun'aqida: an oath concerning a future action (to do or not do something). This is the 'binding' oath mentioned in the Quran (al-Ma'idah 5:89). If one breaks such an oath (hanith), expiation becomes obligatory, as detailed in the verse: feeding ten poor people, clothing them, freeing a slave, or fasting three days if unable. The author cites the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) swearing not to carry the Ash'aris as an example of a future-binding oath, and the hadith about expiation when a better alternative appears (which applies to future oaths).

The third type is Yamin Laghw: an oath about a past event where the swearer believes his statement to be true, but it turns out to be false. This is considered unintentional, and there is no sin nor expiation. The verse 'Allah will not call you to account for the unintentional in your oaths' is the primary proof. The author cites al-Sha'bi's statement that such an oath has no sin or kaffarah, and mentions another type of laghw: idle, careless swearing without intent.

The key distinction is intent and time: Ghamus is deliberate falsehood about the past (sinful, no kaffarah), Mun'aqida is about the future (if broken, kaffarah required), and Laghw is mistaken belief about the past (no sin, no kaffarah). The Hanafi stance on Ghamus is supported by Companion traditions, and the dispute with Shafi'i centers on whether kaffarah can remove the sin of deliberate lying.

Key Terms
یمین غموس (yamin ghamus): A false oath sworn knowingly about a past event; it is a major sin that requires repentance but no expiation.
یمین منعقدہ (yamin mun'aqida): A binding oath about a future action; breaking it necessitates expiation.
یمین لغو (yamin laghw): A vain oath about a past event where the swearer believes it to be true but it is false; no sin or expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on Yamin Ghamus (swearing falsely about a past event)?
A) It requires expiation (kaffarah) like a broken future oath.
B) It is a sin but has no expiation; repentance is required.
C) It is permissible if done unintentionally.
D) It is punishable by flogging.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are classified by time and intent: deliberate falsehood about the past is a grave sin but not expiable, while broken future oaths and mistaken past oaths have distinct rulings.

Equality of Deliberate, Coerced, and Forgetful Oaths
Ruling with proof on equality of oath types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that deliberate, coerced, and forgetful oaths are equally binding and justify it with the school's proofs.

Core Idea
Deliberate, coerced, and forgetful oaths are all equally binding and require expiation if broken.

Oaths are serious matters in Islamic law, but what if they are made under coercion or forgetfully? The Hanafi school holds that such oaths are just as binding as deliberate ones. In this passage, the author first distinguishes the non-binding 'laghw' (vain) oath, then affirms the rule for three categories. We will work through the proofs and address the Shafi'i counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Find the sentence stating that the deliberate, coerced, and forgetful are equal. Recognize that this rule applies to oaths about future actions, not laghw.
The ruling is in the second sentence after the laghw definition.
2
Examine the definition of laghw
Understand that laghw oaths are about past matters mistakenly believed to be true. They are excluded from binding oaths.
This sets the boundary for what is binding.
3
Trace the Hanafi proofs
The passage cites athar from Umar and al-Dahhak that vows (oaths) are valid even in jest, and a hadith about marriage, divorce, and revocation being binding in seriousness and jest.
These analogically support oaths.
4
Analyze the coercion dispute
Identify the Shafi'i position that coerced oaths are invalid, based on hadith about divine forgiveness for coercion. Note the Hanafi response using athar from Ibrahim and analogy with vows.
This is the key cross-madhhab ikhtilaf.
5
Apply the ruling
Conclude that in Hanafi fiqh, all three types of oath incur expiation when broken, and the mufta bihi position is binding.
Practical outcome: expiation is wajib even for coerced oaths.

(c) (references) From this hadith it is learned that in marriage, divorce, and revocation, if one jest, it is reality; similarly, if one swears in jest or forgetfully, the oath becomes effective. And if one is coerced to swear, it becomes effective; its proof is this athar: from Ibrahim: 'It is valid; it is only something by which he ransomed himself.' (d) (references) From this athar it is learned that if one is coerced to swear an oath, it becomes effective. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, if one is coerced to swear, it does not become effective. There is a hadith: from Abu Dharr al-Ghifari, the Prophet said: 'Allah has excused for my community error, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced to do.' Another narration from Aisha: the Prophet said: 'There is no divorce and no emancipation in a state of coercion (ighlaq).' (e) (Ibn Hajr notes in hashya: ...)

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the validity of oaths made under different circumstances. The author begins by defining laghw (vain) oaths as those concerning a past matter that the speaker mistakenly believes to be true; such oaths are not punishable. Then the main ruling: deliberate, coerced, and forgetful oaths are all equal (i.e., valid and binding). The Urdu commentary explains that all three incur expiation if broken, citing athar from Umar and al-Dahhak that vows (including oaths) are serious even in jest. The author then addresses the specific case of divorce, marriage, and revocation, citing a hadith that these are binding whether serious or joking, and analogously oaths. For coercion, an athar from Ibrahim confirms validity. The Hanafi position is contrasted with Imam al-Shafi'i, who considers coerced oaths invalid based on hadiths about forgiveness for error and coercion. The Hanafi response, implicit in the athar and the analogy with divorce, is that coercion does not negate the oath's effectiveness.

Key Terms
لغو (laghw): An oath about a past matter that the speaker mistakenly believes to be true; such an oath is not sinful and does not require expiation.
القاصد (al-qasid): One who deliberately swears an oath knowingly.
المكره (al-mukrah): One who is forced to swear under duress.
الناسي (al-nasi): One who swears forgetfully, without recollection of the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The oath is valid but expiation is not required.
D) The oath is valid only if the person intended it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, deliberate, coerced, and forgetful oaths are all equally binding: breaking any of them incurs wajib expiation.

Doing the Act Sworn Under Coercion or Forgetfulness
Ruling that breaking an oath under coercion or forgetfulness still requires expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that expiation (kaffarah) is wajib when an oath is broken under coercion or forgetfulness, and identify the qiyas-based proof used to support it.

Core Idea
Breaking a valid oath under coercion or forgetfulness still requires expiation.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school: whether breaking an oath while under duress or in a state of forgetfulness triggers the obligation of expiation. The passage presents the Hanafi position alongside the Shafi'i dissent, grounding the ruling in qiyas (analogy) from the validity of the oath itself. Understanding this ruling is crucial for correctly applying the laws of oaths in real-life scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage is ruling upon: the act of doing the thing sworn against (al-mah'luf alayh) when the doer is coerced or forgetful. The ruling is that expiation (kaffarah) is still wajib, despite the lack of intent.
The ruling applies equally to coercion and forgetfulness; both are treated the same.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof the author uses. The passage explicitly mentions qiyas: since the oath itself is valid when sworn under coercion or forgetfulness (as established in an earlier ruling), the same reasoning applies to breaking it. The Shafi'i proof (hadith and verse) is also mentioned but refuted.
The dalil is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) based on the earlier ruling about the validity of the oath.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the analogy works: the oath is valid because the intention to swear is present even under coercion/forgetfulness (according to Hanafi). Therefore, the obligation to fulfill the oath remains, and breaking it incurs expiation. The wajh is the common illa: the validity of the oath itself.
The Shafi'i view sees coercion/forgetfulness as removing liability, analogous to divorce or sin.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the opposing school: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that no expiation is required. Their proof includes a hadith about forgiveness for error and the verse 'There is no blame upon you for what you do in error' (33:5).
The Hanafi response is that these texts apply to sins or specific contexts like divorce, not contractual obligations like oaths.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The author affirms the Hanafi position as mufta bihi. The tarjih is based on consistency: since the oath itself is binding under coercion/forgetfulness, its consequence (expiation) must follow. No further tarjih authority is cited in this passage.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is clear: Hanafi requires expiation, Shafi'i does not.

[۲۶۵۱) (۸) ومن فعل المحلوف عليه مكرها او ناسيا فهو سواء و ۲۶۵۲ (۹) واليمين بالله تعالى او باسم من اسمائه كالرحمن والرحيم او بصفة من صفات ذاته كعزة الله ماجہ شریف، باب طلاق المتزه والناسی ص ۲۹۳ نمبر ۲۰۴۶/۲۰۴۳ رسنن للبیہقی ، باب ما جاء فی طلاق المکتزوج سابع ص ۵۸۵ نمبر ۱۵۰۹۷) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ زبردستی قسم کھلوائی ہو یا طلاق دلوائی ہو تو اس کا اعتبار نہیں ہے اور نہ واقع ہوگی ۔ پہلی حدیث سے یہ بھی ثابت ہوا کہ بھول میں طلاق دی ہو تو واقع نہیں ہوگی اس پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے بھول کر قسم کھالی تو اس کا اعتبار نہیں ہے (۳) آیت بھی ہے۔ ولیس عليكم جناح فيما اخطأتم به (الف) ( آیت ۵ سورۃ الاحزاب (۳۳) اس آیت سے بھی پتا چلا کہ بھول میں قسم کھا لے تو اس کا اعتبار نہیں ہے۔ البتہ کون سی قسم بھول کر کھائی اور کون سی قسم جان کر کھائی اس کا تمیز کرنا بہت مشکل ہے۔ [۲۶۵۱) (۸) کسی نے مخلوف علیہ زبردستی میں کر لیا یا بھول کر لیا تو برا بر ہیں۔ جس بات کے نہ کرنے کی قسم کھائی اس کام کے کرنے پر زبردستی کی جس سے مجبور ہو کر کر لیا تو بھی قسم کا کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ اسی طرح بھول کر وہ کام کر لیا جس کے نہ کرنے کی قسم کھائی ہے تو کفار ملازم ہوگا۔ مسئلہ نمبرے میں گزر گیا کہ بھول کر یا ز بر دستی قسم کھائی ہے تو اس کا اعتبار ہے اسی پر قیاس کر کے بھول کر یا ز بر دستی مخلوف علیہ کام کو کر لیا تو کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ یہاں بھی امام شافعی کے نزدیک یہ ہے کہ بھول میں یا ز بر دستی کرا کر کام کروالیا تو اس پر کفارہ لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ اوپر والی حدیث اور اثر ان کی دلیل ہے۔ مخلوف علیہ : جس بات پر قسم کھالی ہو اس کو مخلوف علیہ کہتے ہیں۔
[2651] (8) Whoever does the thing sworn upon under coercion or forgetfully, it is the same. And [2652] (9) The oath is by Allah Most High or by one of His names like al-Rahman, al-Rahim, or by an attribute of His essence like the Might of Allah. (references) From this hadith it is learned that if an oath or divorce is obtained under coercion, it is not valid and does not occur. From the first hadith it is also proven that if divorce is given forgetfully, it does not occur; by analogy, if an oath is sworn forgetfully, it is not valid. (3) There is also a verse: 'There is no blame upon you for what you do in error.' (Surah al-Ahzab 5) From this verse it is also understood that if one swears forgetfully, it is not valid. However, distinguishing which oath is forgetful and which is deliberate is very difficult. [2651] (8) If someone does the thing sworn upon under coercion or forgetfully, they are equal. Regarding the act he swore not to do, if he was coerced to do it, then expiation is still obligatory. Similarly, if he did it forgetfully, expiation is obligatory. It has passed in the earlier ruling that if an oath is sworn forgetfully or under coercion, it is valid; by analogy, if he does the act forgetfully or under coercion, expiation is obligatory. Here also, according to Imam al-Shafi'i, if he does it forgetfully or under coercion, expiation is not obligatory. The above hadith and athar are their evidence. Mah'luf alayh: The thing upon which the oath was taken is called mah'luf alayh.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings: (8) that breaking an oath by doing the forbidden act under coercion or forgetfulness still requires expiation, and (9) that valid oaths are by Allah, His names, or attributes of His essence. The commentary first discusses the proof for the Shafi'i view that coerced and forgetful oaths are invalid, citing hadiths and a Quranic verse. The author then affirms the Hanafi position that expiation is obligatory regardless of coercion or forgetfulness, drawing an analogy (qiyas) with the earlier ruling that the oath itself is valid under these circumstances. The term 'mah'luf alayh' is defined. The passage also includes a brief note that distinguishing forgetful from deliberate oaths is difficult. The Hanafi view is upheld against the Shafi'i position.

Key Terms
المحلوف عليه (al-mah'luf alayh): The thing or action upon which an oath is taken, especially the act that one swears not to do.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for breaking a valid oath, typically feeding or clothing ten poor persons, or freeing a slave, or fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears not to eat a certain food, but then eats it because he is coerced, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) No expiation because he was coerced.
B) He must perform expiation (kaffarah).
C) His oath is invalid and no expiation.
D) He must repent but no expiation.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi madhhab, breaking a valid oath under coercion or forgetfulness still requires expiation, based on qiyas from the oath's validity itself.

Valid Forms of Oath: By Allah, His Names, and Attributes
Ruling on valid oath formulas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid forms of oath in Hanafi fiqh and explain why swearing by other entities is invalid.

Core Idea
An oath is only valid if it invokes Allah, His name, or an essential attribute – no other formula counts.

The Hanafi school strictly limits valid oaths to those sworn by Allah, His names, or His essential attributes. This ruling, derived from the Prophet's practice, ensures that oaths carry the proper weight of divine invocation. In this lesson, we examine the exact forms and the underlying proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three valid categories
List the categories: (1) the name 'Allah', (2) any of His names, (3) an attribute of His essence.
Each category is equally valid.
2
Distinguish names from attributes
Names like al-Rahman are proper names; attributes like 'Might' describe His essence. Both are valid.
Attributes of action (e.g., 'Creator') are not included here; only essential attributes.
3
Recall the hadith proof
The Prophet used 'By Allah' (والله) in a hadith, showing this formula validates an oath.
This is a hadith ahad, but sufficient for proof.
4
Note what is excluded
Swearing by the Prophet, the Ka'ba, or honor is not a valid oath in the Hanafi school.
Such statements have no legal consequence.

(9) The oath is by Allah Most High or by one of His names like al-Rahman, al-Rahim, or by an attribute of His essence like the Might of Allah. From this hadith it is learned that if an oath or divorce is obtained under coercion, it is not valid and does not occur. From the first hadith it is also proven that if divorce is given forgetfully, it does not occur; by analogy, if an oath is sworn forgetfully, it is not valid. (3) There is also a verse: 'There is no blame upon you for what you do in error.' (Surah al-Ahzab 5) From this verse it is also understood that if one swears forgetfully, it is not valid. However, distinguishing which oath is forgetful and which is deliberate is very difficult. Regarding the method of swearing: [2652] (1) It is by the word Allah or by one of His names like al-Rahman, al-Rahim, or by an attribute of His essence like the Might of Allah, His Majesty, or His Grandeur. If one wishes to swear, he should swear by the word Allah or by one of His names, or by an attribute of Allah's essence, e.g., 'By the Might of Allah,' 'By His Majesty,' or 'By His Grandeur'; in this way, the oath is concluded. There is proof in the hadith: from Aisha from the Prophet: 'O Ummah of Muhammad, by Allah, if you knew what I know, you would laugh little and weep much.' (b) (references) In this hadith, the phrase 'By Allah' is used, which is an oath by the essence of Allah. Footnote (a): There is no blame on you for what you do by mistake. (b) The Prophet said: O Ummah of Muhammad, by Allah, if you knew what I know, you would weep much and laugh little.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage states the ruling (9) that a valid oath must be sworn by Allah, one of His names (e.g., al-Rahman, al-Rahim), or an attribute of His essence (e.g., Might, Majesty, Grandeur). The Urdu commentary explains the method and provides a hadith as proof where the Prophet said 'By Allah'. The passage also includes a lengthy digression on the Shafi'i view about coercion and forgetfulness, but that appears to be a continuation of the previous lesson's discussion. The core ruling is straightforward: an oath is only contractually binding if it invokes Allah, His names, or essential attributes. Swearing by other things (e.g., the Prophet, the Ka'ba) is not a valid oath in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah, His names, or attributes, which is legally binding.
صفات الذات (sifat al-dhat): Attributes of Allah's essence, such as Might, Majesty, Grandeur; swearing by these constitutes a valid oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Validity of Oaths by Allah's Attributes: Essential vs. Relational
Ruling with proof: validity of oaths by Allah's essential attributes and invalidity by knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and relational attributes of Allah for oath validity and apply the Hanafi ruling that swearing by essential attributes contracts an oath while swearing by knowledge does not.

Core Idea
Only essential attributes of Allah contract an oath; relational attributes like knowledge do not.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on which attributes of Allah can be used to form a valid oath. The source passage provides evidence from the Prophet's practice and the prohibition of swearing by fathers to establish that oaths must be by Allah or His essential attributes. We will explore the distinction between essential (dhatiyyah) and relational attributes and the specific case of 'ilm Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Swearing by Allah's attributes. The hukm: valid if essential, invalid if relational.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites hadiths from Ibn Umar, Abu Huraira, Abu Dharr, and the prohibition of swearing by fathers.
These are ahad hadiths but considered reliable.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's practice of swearing by attributes like 'Mukallib al-Qulub' and 'Izzah' shows permissibility. The prohibition of swearing by fathers implies only swearing by Allah or His attributes is valid.
Focus on the linguistic and contextual evidence.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider swearing by any attribute of Allah valid, but Hanafis distinguish essential from relational.
This is a legitimate scholarly difference.
5
Apply the distinction
Identify whether an attribute is essential (e.g., 'Izzah, Rubbubiyyah) or relational (e.g., 'Ilm referring to ma'lum). Determine the hukm.
Essential attributes are inseparable from Allah's essence.

The proof for swearing by an attribute of Allah is this hadith: From Ibn Umar: 'The oath of the Prophet, peace be upon him, used to be: No, and the Overturner of hearts' (Bukhari no. 6628). In this hadith, the attribute of Allah 'Overturner of hearts' is used to swear. (3) Prophet Ayyub swore by Allah's honor. A piece of the hadith: From Abu Huraira from the Prophet... his Lord called him: 'O Ayyub, did I not enrich you from what you see?' He said: 'Yes, by Your honor, but I have no need from Your blessing.' In this hadith, 'by Your honor' is swearing by Allah's honor. The proof for swearing by another name of Allah is this hadith: From Abu Dharr: 'I reached him saying under the shadow of the Ka'ba: The losers are the ones, by the Lord of the Ka'ba.' In this hadith, 'by the Lord of the Ka'ba' is a descriptive name of Allah by which he swore. From this it is known that one can swear by Allah's descriptive name. (2653 (10)) But the saying of someone: 'By the knowledge of Allah' is not an oath. If someone swears by Allah's knowledge, the oath is not contracted. Here, by 'knowledge', the known thing is meant, and the known thing is not an essential attribute of Allah, so swearing by Allah's knowledge does not contract an oath. And in a hadith: 'If one swears by Allah's name or by an essential attribute, the oath is contracted.' Hadith: From Abdullah ibn Umar: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, found Umar ibn al-Khattab traveling in a caravan swearing by his father, and he said: 'Indeed Allah forbids you to swear by your fathers. Whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or be silent.' From this hadith it is known that one should swear by Allah or be silent, because swearing by something else does not contract an oath. Footnote: (a) The Prophet's oath used to be: 'No, and the Overturner of hearts.' (b) Transmitted from the Prophet: The Lord of Ayyub called: 'O Ayyub, did I not make you independent from what you see?' He said: 'Yes, by Your honor, but I am not free from Your blessing.' (c) Abu Dharr said: 'I reached him, he was saying under the shadow of the Ka'ba: 'By the Lord of the Ka'ba, he is the loser.' (d) He found Umar walking in a caravan swearing by his father, so he said: 'Listen, Allah forbids you from swearing by your fathers. Whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or be silent.'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Oaths. It establishes that swearing by Allah's essential attributes (like His majesty, honor, or descriptive names such as 'Overturner of hearts' and 'Lord of the Ka'ba') is valid and contracts an oath. However, swearing by Allah's knowledge ('ilm) does not contract an oath because 'knowledge' here refers to the known object (ma'lum), which is not an essential attribute of Allah. The hadith evidence includes the Prophet's practice, the story of Prophet Ayyub, and the prohibition of swearing by fathers, all showing that valid oaths must be by Allah or His essential attributes. The footnote explains the hadith references.

Key Terms
صفة ذاتية (sifah dhatiyyah): An essential attribute of Allah that is inseparable from His essence, such as His honor, majesty, or descriptive names like 'Lord of the Ka'ba'.
علم اللہ ('ilm Allah): The knowledge of Allah; here interpreted as 'the known thing' (ma'lum), which is not an essential attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a valid oath can only be made by Allah's essential attributes, not by relational attributes like knowledge.

Swearing by Allah's Active Attributes is Not an Oath
Ruling that swearing by active attributes like anger does not constitute an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling that swearing by Allah's active attributes is invalid and EXPLAIN why these attributes do not form a valid oath.

Core Idea
An oath sworn by what is not eternal cannot bind—active attributes of Allah do not count.

In this lesson, we examine a specific rule from the Hanafi Book of Oaths: swearing by Allah's attributes of action, such as His anger or displeasure, does not constitute a valid oath. The reason lies in the Hanafi distinction between essential attributes (sifat dhatiyyah) and active attributes (sifat fi'liyyah). Understanding this distinction is key to knowing which oaths are binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swearing by an active attribute (e.g., 'By the anger of Allah'). The ruling is that the oath is invalid (batil) and does not contract an oath.
This is a clear ruling statement; no proof is cited.
2
Distinguish essential vs. active attributes
Essential attributes (e.g., knowledge, power) are eternal and inherent to Allah's essence. Active attributes (e.g., anger, pleasure) are temporally occurring, related to His actions.
Hanafis treat active attributes differently from essential ones in oath validity.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
Oaths are only valid when sworn by an eternal attribute of Allah. Since active attributes are not eternal, swearing by them does not constitute a valid oath.
The principle is that an oath must be by something that is eternal and uncreated.
4
Note the consequence
The person is not considered an oath-taker (halif), so no legal obligations (like expiation) follow if they break their word.
However, it is still discouraged to use such phrases.
5
Consider cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may consider all divine attributes equivalent for oath purposes, leading to a valid oath. The Hanafi position is specific.
This is a point of ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جلف بصفة من صفات الفعل كغضب الله وسخط الله لم يكن حالفا [۲۶۵۴] (۱) إذا حلف بصفة من صفات الفعل كغضب الله وسخط الله لم يكن حالفا

اگر تم کھائی کسی فعلی صفت کے ساتھ جیسے غضب اللہ یا حظ اللہ تو قسم کھانے والا نہیں ہوگا۔ وہ صفات جواللہ کی ذاتی نہیں ہیں بلکہ فعلی اور وقتی ہیں ان کے واسطے سے قسم کھائے تو قسم منعقد نہیں ہوگی۔ جیسے اللہ کا غضب یا سخط اللہ مثلا
If one swears by an attribute of action like the anger of Allah or the displeasure of Allah, he is not an oath-taker. [2654] (1) If one swears by an attribute of action like the anger of Allah or the displeasure of Allah, he is not an oath-taker. If you swear by an active attribute like the anger of Allah or His displeasure, then the one swearing will not be an oath-taker. Those attributes are not essential to Allah but are active and temporal; swearing by them does not contract an oath. For example, Allah's anger or displeasure.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the Book of Oaths. It clarifies that swearing by Allah's active attributes (sifat al-fi'l), such as anger (ghadab) or displeasure (sukht), does not constitute a valid oath. These attributes are not essential (dhatiyyah) but are temporally occurring in relation to His actions. Therefore, an oath sworn by them is not legally binding. The author notes this as point [2654].

Key Terms
صفة فعل (sifah fi'l): An attribute of Allah that is related to His actions, such as anger or pleasure, which are not essential to His essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a valid oath.
B) It is invalid because anger is not an essential attribute.
C) It is valid only if the person intends it seriously.
D) It is makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
Swearing by Allah's active attributes such as anger or pleasure does not constitute a valid oath in the Hanafi school because these attributes are not eternal and essential.

Swearing by Other Than Allah Is Invalid
Ruling that swearing by other than Allah is invalid, with proof from hadith and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swearing by other than Allah and identify the evidence from hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
An oath is only valid if sworn by Allah; swearing by the Prophet, Quran, or Ka'ba is invalid and considered shirk.

In daily life, people sometimes swear by sacred objects like the Ka'ba or the Quran, thinking it adds solemnity. However, the Hanafi school strictly limits valid oaths to those sworn by Allah Almighty. This lesson examines the ruling, its evidence, and its extension through analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that swearing by other than Allah, such as by the Prophet, Quran, or Ka'ba, does not constitute a valid oath. The ruling is that such an oath is batil (invalid).
The Arabic phrase 'لم يكن حالفا' means 'he is not a swearer' i.e., the oath is void.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith reported by Ibn Umar quotes the Prophet saying: 'Whoever swears by other than Allah has committed shirk.' This is the core textual evidence.
The hadith is from Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi, classified as ahad but with strong indication.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit declaration that such an act is shirk indicates both prohibition and invalidity. The word 'ashraka' (committed shirk) shows the sin and nullifies the oath.
In Hanafi usul, al-amr al-mujarrad 'an al-qarina yufid al-wujub, but here the text is a warning (tahdid), not a command.
4
Apply qiyas to other cases
The text extends the same ruling to swearing by the Prophet or the Quran via qiyas: the common illa is 'swearing by other than Allah'.
The athar from Qatada supports this by declaring swearing by the Quran makruh, reinforcing the general rule.
5
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that any oath not taken by Allah (or His names/attributes) is invalid and sinful. No expiation is required for breaking such an oath.
This is the unanimous view in the Hanafi school (zahir al-riwayah).

Source Text

ومن حلف بغير الله عزوجل لم يكن حالفا كالنبي عليه السلام والقرآن والكعبة

سمع ابن عمر رجلا يحلف لا والكعبة فقال له ابن عمر اني سمعت رسول الله الله يقول من حلف بغير الله فقد اشرک (ب) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب کراہیتہ الحلف بالآباء ص ۰۷ انمبر ۳۲۵۱ / ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء فی ان من حلف بغیر اللہ فقد اشرك ص ۲۷۹ نمبر ۱۵۳۵) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ ایک صحابی کعبہ کی قسم کھا رہا تھا تو حضرت عبد اللہ بن عمر نے فرمایا کہ اس کی قسم مت کھاؤ۔ اگر اللہ کے علاوہ کی قسم کھائی تو اللہ کے ساتھ شرک کیا۔ اس پر قیاس کر کے نبی اور قرآن کی قسم بھی کھائے تو قسم منعقد نہیں ہوگی۔

And whoever swears by other than Allah Almighty is not an oath-taker, such as by the Prophet, peace be upon him, the Quran, and the Ka'ba. Ibn Umar heard a man swearing 'No, by the Ka'ba' and said to him: 'I heard the Messenger of Allah say: Whoever swears by other than Allah has committed shirk.' (Abu Dawud no. 3251; Tirmidhi no. 1535). This hadith states that a companion was swearing by the Ka'ba, and Abdullah ibn Umar said: Do not swear by it. If one swears by other than Allah, he has committed shirk with Allah. By analogy, swearing by the Prophet or the Quran also does not contract an oath. There is an athar: Ma'mar informed us from Qatada who said: It is disliked for a person to swear by emancipation or divorce, and he should only swear by Allah, and he disliked swearing by the mushaf (Quran). (Musannaf Abdurrazzaq no. 15932). This athar declares swearing by the Quran as makruh.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. It states that swearing by anything other than Allah, such as the Prophet Muhammad, the Quran, or the Ka'ba, does not constitute a valid oath. The proof is a hadith where Ibn Umar heard a man swearing by the Ka'ba and informed him that the Prophet said swearing by other than Allah is shirk. The author then extends this ruling by qiyas (analogy) to the Prophet and the Quran. Additionally, an athar from Qatada shows that swearing by the Quran is disliked (makruh). Thus, any oath not by Allah is invalid.

Key Terms
حلف بغير الله (halaf bi ghayr Allah): Swearing an oath by something other than Allah, such as the Prophet, Quran, or Ka'ba, which is invalid according to Hanafi fiqh.

Key Takeaway

The Three Letters of Oath: Waw, Ba, and Ta
Explanation of the three Arabic letters used to form valid oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three letters of oath recognized by the Hanafi school and identify the textual proofs from Quran and Sunnah that establish their validity.

Core Idea
Only waw, ba, and ta form valid oaths when prefixed to Allah's name.

Oaths are serious legal acts in Islam. The Hanafi school specifies three Arabic letters—waw, ba, and ta—that, when placed before the name of Allah, create a binding oath. This lesson examines these letters and the evidence that supports their use.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: the use of specific Arabic letters (waw, ba, ta) to form an oath. The ruling is that an oath using any of these three letters before 'Allah' is valid (sahih) and contracts the oath.
The text explicitly lists the letters.
2
Locate the proofs
Find the dalil cited: the hadith of Abu Bakr mentioning all three forms, the hadith of Aisha using 'Wallah', and the Quranic verse using 'Tallah'. These are the primary textual evidences.
Three proofs are given: two hadith and one Quranic verse.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Examine how each proof demonstrates the ruling. The explicit use of these letters in the speech of the Prophet’s companions and in the Quran establishes their validity as oath particles.
The proof is direct usage (dalil 'aqli naqli).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Check for disagreement. The text indicates no ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school; the ruling is consistent across the madhhab. Cross-madhhab, the validity of these letters is generally agreed upon.
The Hanafi position is unanimous on this point.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Since there is no dispute, no tarjih is needed. The ruling is established and relied upon (mufta bihi) from the zahir al-riwayah.
The ruling is clear and authoritative.

تم حروف قسم سے ہوتی ہے اور حروف قسم تین ہیں واو جیسے واللہ اور بہا سے جیسے باللہ اور تا سے جیسے تاللہ ۔ عربی میں اللہ کے پہلے واد یا با یا تالے آئے تب بھی قسم منعقد ہو جاتی ہے۔ حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ قال ابو بكر عند النبي الا لا اله الا ها الله اذا يقال والله ، وبالله ، وتالله (و) ( بخاری شریف کیف کان سمین النبی ص ۹۸۰ نمبر ۶۶۲۸) اس اثر میں واللہ، باللہ اور تاللہ تینوں حرفوں کا ثبوت ہے (۲) عن عائشة ... يا امة محمد والله لو تعلمون (ه) ( بخاری شریف، نمبر ۶۶۳۱) میں واو کا ثبوت ہے۔ اور تا کے ساتھ قسم کھانے کا ثبوت اس آیت میں ہے۔ وتا اللہ لاکیدن اصنامكم بعد ان تولوا مدبرين ( آیت ۵۷ سورۃ الانبیاء ۲۱) اس آیت میں تا کے ساتھ قسم کھائی ہے۔

Evidence for this is in the hadith: Abu Bakr said in the presence of the Prophet: 'There is no god but Allah, it is said: Wallah, Billah, and Tallah.' (Bukhari no. 6628) This athar proves the three letters. (2) From Aisha: 'O Ummah of Muhammad, by Allah (Wallah) if you knew...' (Bukhari no. 6631) proves waw. And proof for swearing with ta is in this verse: 'And by Allah (ta Allah), I will certainly plot against your idols after you turn your backs.' (Surah al-Anbiya 21:57) In this verse, swearing with ta is used.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary

Any oath using these letters before Allah's name is valid and contracts an oath.

The Invalidity of Oaths by Attributes of Action
Ruling on swearing by ghadab or sukht of Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths made by attributes of action and identify why such oaths are invalid.

Core Idea
An oath by a created attribute of Allah is no oath at all.

In this lesson, we examine a concise ruling from the Hanafi matn: swearing by attributes of action such as Allah's anger or displeasure does not constitute a valid oath. The key distinction lies in the nature of the attribute — whether it is eternal (sifat al-dhat) or created (sifat al-fi'l). This ruling has practical implications for anyone making vows or oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act? Swearing by an attribute of action. Ruling: not a valid oath.
2
Distinguish attributes of action from attributes of essence
The ruling hinges on this distinction. Attributes of action are created, not eternal.
Hanafi scholars classify Allah's attributes into sifat al-dhat (eternal) and sifat al-fi'l (created). Oaths are valid only by the former.
3
Determine the consequence
The oath is not contracted at all; it is as if never uttered.
4
Extend to other similar attributes
This applies to any attribute of action like anger, displeasure, coming, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جلف بصفة من صفات الفعل كغضب الله وسخط الله لم يكن حالفا
If one swears by an attribute of action such as the anger of Allah or the displeasure of Allah, he is not a swearer.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn. It addresses swearing by attributes of Allah that are not His essence or eternal attributes, but rather attributes of action (sifat al-fi'l) like ghadab (anger) and sukht (displeasure). According to the Hanafi school, such oaths are not valid because the oath is only effective when taken by Allah's essence or His eternal attributes (sifat al-dhat). The commentator explains that this is because attributes of action are created and contingent, not eternal, and thus swearing by them does not carry the same weight as swearing by Allah Himself.

Key Terms
صفات الفعل (sifat al-fi'l): Attributes of Allah that are related to His actions and are created, e.g., anger, pleasure, coming, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No, because anger is a sin.

Key Takeaway
An oath by a created attribute of Allah (sifat al-fi'l) is invalid; only oaths by Allah's name or eternal attributes are binding.

The Invalidity of Swearing by Other Than Allah
Ruling on swearing by prophet, Quran, Kaaba with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swearing by other than Allah and explain the prophetic evidence that supports it.

Core Idea
An oath sworn by other than Allah is invalid and sinful — only oaths by Allah contract.

This lesson addresses a common practice: swearing by the Prophet, the Quran, or the Kaaba. The Hanafi school rules that such oaths do not form a valid contract at all. We will examine the textual proofs from hadith and understand why this ruling is upheld despite differing views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is swearing by something other than Allah (e.g., Prophet, Quran, Kaaba). The ruling is that the oath is invalid; the swearer is not considered a swearer (lam yakun halifan).
This is a foundational ruling in the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Two hadith are cited: (1) 'Whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or remain silent' (Bukhari/Muslim) and (2) 'Whoever swears by other than Allah has committed shirk' (Abu Dawud/Tirmidhi).
Both hadith are ahad, but their meaning is clear.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof proves)
The first hadith restricts valid oaths to swearing by Allah; any other formula is not an oath. The second hadith equates swearing by other than Allah to shirk, indicating prohibition and invalidity.
The Hanafi interpretation takes the literal meaning: only Allah can be sworn by.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that swearing by the Quran or the Prophet can form a valid oath if intended as an oath.
This difference stems from different usul regarding the definition of qasam.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preferred view)
The Hanafi position is relied upon because the hadith's apparent meaning is general and unconditional; the Shafi'i view is considered weak due to lack of direct textual support.
The mufta bihi ruling is that the oath does not contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حلف بغير الله عزوجل لم يكن حالفا كالنبي عليه السلام والقرآن والكعبة
And whoever swears by other than Allah Almighty is not a swearer, such as by the Prophet (peace be upon him), the Quran, and the Kaaba.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. The matn states that swearing by anyone or anything other than Allah does not constitute a valid oath. Examples include the Prophet, the Quran, and the Kaaba. The commentary then provides two hadith proofs: (a) 'Whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or remain silent' (Bukhari and Muslim), and (b) the story of Ibn Umar who heard a man swearing by the Kaaba and said, 'Whoever swears by other than Allah has committed shirk' (Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi). The second hadith indicates that swearing by the Kaaba is akin to shirk. By qiyas, the same applies to swearing by the Prophet or the Quran. Additionally, an athar from Qatada indicates dislike (karaha) of swearing by the Quran. The Hanafi position is that such oaths are not formed (la yan'aqid).

Key Terms
حلف بغير الله (halafa bighayri Allah): To swear by something other than Allah, such as the Prophet, Quran, Kaaba, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The only valid oath in the Hanafi school is one sworn by Allah; oaths by other than Allah do not contract and are sinful.

The Ruling on Implicit Oaths in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on omitted oath particles with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on implicit oaths and identify the forms of oath expressions that are valid without explicit particles.

Core Idea
Omitting the oath particle still binds you — Hanafi fiqh validates the oath by intention.

In everyday speech, people sometimes swear by saying 'Allah, I will do such-and-such' without the 'waw' particle. Is such an utterance a binding oath? The Hanafi school, based on common Arabic usage, considers these expressions valid oaths. This lesson examines the rule of idmar al-huruf (implicit particles) and the use of taa' in oath formulas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is uttering an oath without explicit particle. The ruling: the oath is valid (sahih) and binding.
2
Recognize the forms
Two forms mentioned: using taa' (tallahi) and omitting the particle entirely (Allah instead of Wallah).
3
Understand the basis
The basis is common Arabic usage ('urf) and intention (niyyah). The speaker intends an oath, so the omitted particle is implied.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools like Shafi'i may require the explicit particle for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتاء كقوله تالله وقد تضمر الحروف فيكون حالفا كقوله الله لا افعل كذا
And the taa' is like saying 'tallaahi', and sometimes the letters are omitted so he becomes an oath-taker like saying 'Allaahi, I will not do such-and-such'.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage states two rulings: first, that the letter taa' can be used in oath expressions like 'tallaahi'. Second, that oath particles can be omitted, yet the oath is still valid, as in the phrase 'Allaahi la af'alu kadha' (By Allah, I will not do such-and-such), where the waw is implied. The author explains that in Arabic, when someone says 'Allaahu' with the intention of an oath, the particle is understood. This is a basic principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding implicit oaths.

Key Terms
إضمار الحروف (idmar al-huruf): Omitting the explicit oath particle (like waw) while still intending an oath, which is considered a valid oath in Hanafi school.
تالله (tallahi): An oath form using the letter taa' instead of waw, meaning 'by Allah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Only if he says 'Wallahi' explicitly.
D) Only if he also says 'tallaahi'.

Key Takeaway

Valid Oath Expressions: Aqsam, Ahlef, Ashhad
Ruling on valid oath expressions (Aqsam, Ahlef, Ashhad) with dalil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three verbal expressions that constitute a valid oath in Hanafi fiqh and identify the proof from hadith and Quran.

Core Idea
Aqsam, Ahlef, Ashhad — each is a valid oath even without 'billah'.

In Hanafi fiqh, an oath is not restricted to the phrase 'Wallahi'. This passage from a classical text lists three verbal forms—Aqsam, Ahlef, Ashhad—with or without mentioning Allah, as sufficient for a binding oath. The author supports this with a hadith and a Quranic verse, demonstrating the school's reliance on textual evidence for leniency in oath phrasing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Expressions
The text mentions three Arabic verbs: أقسم (aqsam), أحلف (ahlef), and أشهد (ashhad). Each can be used alone or followed by بالله (billah).
2
Recognize the Inclusion of 'billah'
Both versions—with and without 'billah'—are valid. The key is the verbal form itself.
3
Examine the First Proof: Hadith
The Prophet (SAW) acknowledged 'أقسمت عليك يا رسول الله' as an oath, showing that 'aqsam' alone suffices.
Hadith from Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah.
4
Examine the Second Proof: Quran
Allah says in Surah al-Munafiqun (2): 'اتخذوا أيمانهم جنة' – 'They have taken their oaths as a shield.' The verse uses 'ayman' (oaths) in reference to hypocrites who said 'nashhad' (we bear witness), indicating that 'ashhad' functions as an oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا قال أقسم او أقسم بالله او احلف او احلف بالله او اشهد او اشهد بالله فهو حالف. والدليل حديث: 'اقسمت عليك يا رسول الله' وقوله تعالى: 'اتخذوا أيمانهم جنة' (المنافقون: 2).
And if he says 'Aqsam' (I swear) or 'Aqsam billah' (I swear by Allah), or 'Ahlef' (I take an oath) or 'Ahlef billah' (I take an oath by Allah), or 'Ashhad' (I bear witness) or 'Ashhad billah' (I bear witness by Allah), he is an oath-taker. The evidence is the hadith: 'I ask you by oath, O Messenger of Allah' and the verse: 'They have taken their oaths as a shield' (al-Munafiqun: 2).
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage enumerates three verbal forms that constitute valid oaths even without explicitly saying 'Wallahi': (1) Aqsam or Aqsam billah, (2) Ahlef or Ahlef billah, (3) Ashhad or Ashhad billah. The author provides two proofs: a hadith where someone said 'Aqsamtu alayka' (I swear by you) to the Prophet, which the Prophet acknowledged as a valid oath, and the Quranic verse 'They have taken their oaths as a shield' (al-Munafiqun 2), where the word 'shahada' (probably 'nashhad') is interpreted as an oath. The commentary also cites an athar (report) from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that the early scholars forbade swearing by 'shahada' and 'ahd', indicating they are valid oath forms. Thus, these three expressions are sufficient to create a binding oath in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
أقسم (aqsam): To swear an oath; considered a valid oath expression in Hanafi fiqh.
حلف (halafa): To take an oath; the verbal noun 'hilf' is used for oaths.
شهد (shahida): To bear witness; in this context, 'ashhad' used as an oath expression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, which of the following expressions DOES NOT form a valid oath?
A) أقسم بالله
B) أحلف بالله
C) أشهد بالله
D) أمن بالله

Key Takeaway
Three verbal expressions—Aqsam, Ahlef, Ashhad—are sufficient to create a binding Hanafi oath, supported by hadith and Quran.

Ruling on 'Ahd Allah' and 'Mithaq Allah' as Valid Oaths
Ruling that 'Ahd Allah' and 'Mithaq Allah' are valid oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that 'Ahd Allah' and 'Mithaq Allah' are valid oath expressions and explain the Quranic evidence that supports this.

Core Idea
Swearing by the covenant or pledge of Allah is a binding oath, just like swearing by Allah Himself.

This lesson establishes that oaths are not limited to the words 'Wallahi'. The Hanafi school includes 'Ahd Allah' (covenant of Allah) and 'Mithaq Allah' (pledge of Allah) as valid oath expressions, citing Quranic proof that equates covenant with oaths. Understanding this expands the scope of binding verbal commitments in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is uttering 'Ahd Allah' or 'Mithaq Allah' as an oath. The ruling is that this counts as a valid, binding oath (yamin).
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites Quran 16:91: 'And fulfill the covenant of Allah when you have made a covenant, and do not break the oaths after their confirmation.'
The verse is quoted in the source to establish the link.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse pairs 'covenant of Allah' (ahd Allah) with 'oaths' (ayman), showing they are treated alike. This indicates that 'ahd' is a form of oath in Islamic legal context.
The imperative to fulfill the covenant implies binding nature.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require explicit 'yamin' wording or intention for an oath. Thus, this is a mukhtalaf fih issue.
Hanafi view is based on linguistic and textual equivalence.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in Hanafi fatwa is that 'Ahd Allah' and 'Mithaq Allah' form valid oaths, as stated in Zahir al-Riwayah.
No intra-Hanafi disagreement is reported.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك قوله وعهد الله وميثاقه فهو يمين على نذر او نذر الله. والدليل قوله تعالى: 'وأوفوا بعهد الله إذا عاهدتم ولا تنقضوا الأيمان بعد توكيدها' (النحل: 91).
And similarly, his saying 'By the covenant of Allah' and 'By His pledge' is an oath upon a vow or a vow to Allah. And the evidence is His saying: 'And fulfill the covenant of Allah when you have made a covenant, and do not break the oaths after their confirmation' (Surah an-Nahl: 91).
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage states that phrases like 'By the covenant of Allah' (عهد الله) and 'By the pledge of Allah' (ميثاق الله) are considered valid oaths in Hanafi fiqh. The author draws on Quranic evidence from Surah an-Nahl (16:91), where fulfilling the covenant of Allah is equated with fulfilling oaths (الأيمان). The verse says: 'Fulfill the covenant of Allah when you make a covenant, and do not break the oaths after their confirmation.' This indicates that 'ahd' (covenant) is synonymous with 'yamin' (oath) in legal context. Similarly, 'mithaq' (pledge) carries the same weight. Therefore, if someone says 'I swear by the covenant of Allah that I will do such-and-such', a binding oath is created, and breaking it requires expiation (kaffarah).

Key Terms
عهد الله (ahd allah): The covenant of Allah; in this context, used as an oath expression.
ميثاق الله (mithaq allah): The pledge of Allah; synonym of 'ahd' and used as an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because it does not say 'Wallahi'.
B) Yes, because 'Ahd Allah' is considered an oath.
C) Only if he also says 'Wallahi'.
D) No, because 'Ahd' is not an oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, swearing by the covenant or pledge of Allah creates a valid, binding oath supported by Quran 16:91.

Phrases Constituting an Oath: Non-Muslim Affiliation and Vows
Compilation of rulings and proofs for phrases that create an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that specific expressions (e.g., 'I am a Jew', 'upon me is a vow') form a binding oath, identify the supporting proofs (Quran, hadith, athar), and apply the ruling that kaffarah yamin is wajib upon breaking such an oath.

Core Idea
Declaring 'I am a Jew' as a condition makes an oath, not apostasy.

In this passage, the author compiles a list of phrases that, when used conditionally in Hanafi fiqh, create a legally binding oath (yamin). These include expressions of non-Muslim affiliation (e.g., 'I am a Jew') and vows (nadhr). The lesson demonstrates how the school uses Quranic verses, hadiths, and athar (companion reports) to establish that such phrases trigger the obligation of kaffarah (expiation) upon breaking the condition. This reflects the Hanafi principle of treating implicit oath expressions with the same force as explicit ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expressions and the ruling
List the expressions mentioned: 'I am a Jew/Christian/Magian/idolater/disbeliever', 'upon me is the covenant of Allah', 'upon me is a vow', etc. The ruling: each is a valid oath; if the condition is broken, kaffarah yamin is wajib.
These are not actual changes in religion – only legal oath consequences.
2
Locate the primary proofs
The author cites Quran 13:20 (praising those who fulfill 'ahd' and 'mithaq'), hadiths equating vow expiation to oath expiation (from Ibn Abbas and Uqbah ibn Amir), and athar from Ibrahim enumerating the phrases. Identify the type of each proof (qat'i/zanni thubut and dalala).
The Quran verse is used to show that 'ahd' and 'mithaq' are synonymous with oath.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah (how each proof supports the ruling)
For the Quran: the verse treats 'ahd' and 'mithaq' as binding, so saying 'upon me is the covenant of Allah' creates an obligation. For the hadiths: they directly equate the expiation of a vow to that of an oath, so vows are legally oaths. For the athar: the comprehensive list confirms that all these expressions are considered oaths in Hanafi practice.
The hadith from Uqbah is more explicit: 'The expiation of a vow is the expiation of an oath.'
4
Understand the key distinction
The author emphasises that saying 'I am a Jew' under oath does not make one an actual Jew or apostate – the person remains Muslim and only incurs a kaffarah obligation. This is supported by the hadith: 'Whoever swears by a religion other than Islam falsely...' and the athar from Kharijah.
Actual apostasy requires explicit denial of Islam, not just an oath phrase.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi and authority
Imam Muhammad concludes: 'wa bihadha kullihi na'khudh' (we take by all of this), making the position zahir al-riwayah. All listed expressions are operative for fatwa.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih; some schools do not treat these as binding oaths.

Source Text
